<question>



What is the history of "Victorian red brick"?



how would you describe its colour and is this shade the most popular of clay found in the UK??



or where did most of it originate from there?



which specific area?



Thanks for your answers!



</question>



<additional details>



which famous buildings and where  can you name with this ?



</additional details>



<answer from>



EarthCal...
Member since: 08 July 2006
Total points: 5874 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Jeez you are in trouble with this examination question. Good luck anyway.



</answer>



<answer from>



quokka
Member since: 10 August 2006
Total points: 1451 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Red brick (or "redbrick") is a term used to refer to the six civic British universities founded in the major industrial cities of England in the Victorian era which achieved university status before World War II. Modern usage of the term also sometimes refers to the members of the Russell Group of universities founded between 1850 and 1960, although the terms are by no means mutually inclusive.



</answer>



<answer from>



caroline ♥
Member since: 21 December 2006
Total points: 32378 (Level 7)



Contributing In:



Glasgow



</answer from>



<answer>



The fired colour of clay bricks is significantly influenced by the chemical and mineral content of raw materials, the firing temperature and the atmosphere in the kiln. For example pink coloured bricks are the result of a high iron content, white or yellow bricks have a higher lime content. Most bricks burn to various red hues, if the temperature is increased the colour moves through dark red, purple and then to brown or grey at around 1300 °C. Calcium silicate bricks have a wider range of shades and colours, depending on the colorants used.



</answer>

<answer>



Get a train from the Luton airport to the London. Get out and hail a taxi. Get in the taxi and say "Astor Hotel, Queensborough Terrace my good fellow - and don't spare the horses". Get out on arrival. You have reached your destination.



Look - whoever gave me the thumsdown - I was born in London, I have known London for half a century and I can assure you that the above would be the most stress free and safe way for a foreign visitor to find a particular hotel.

<answer>



Avoid Edinburgh at all costs, nothing Scottish about the place and it’s just a rip-off.



The best castile in Scotland to visit it, by far, Stirling Castle.



Glasgow, the largest city in Scotland, offers fabulous shopping, crowded pubs and clubs and terrific restaurants. Art lovers will also find plenty to capture their imagination, as the city is home to more than 30 galleries and museums, including the world famous Burrell Collection and Saint Mungo Museum of Religious Life and Art. Naturally it is also possible to visit the coast from Glasgow or discover the rich history of the region by exploring neighbouring cities and villages.



Architecture: Many of the city’s buildings date back to the 19th century, when Glasgow was the trading centre of Scotland. Nowadays, the city palaces of yesteryear serve as offices and department stores. Enjoying both architecture and the contents of the buildings is the theme of The Lighthouse in the heart of Glasgow, with a variety of dynamic exhibitions on art and architecture.



Shopping: Glasgow is the largest shopping city of Great Britain after London and offers excellent shopping. Visit the pedestrianised shopping precincts Sauchiehall Street, Buchanan Street and Argyle Street or one of the indoor shopping malls, such as the Buchanan Galleries or St Enoch Centre.



Food and drink: Whisky, preferably single malt, is the drink of choice in Scotland. A visit to a distillery is certainly worthwhile. Like in England, the Scots enjoy a hearty breakfast with bacon and eggs, whereas dinner usually consists of a wide selection of meats. Salmon is also widely available. Mutton lovers must certainly try the traditional Haggis.



Getting Around: Glasgow has one of the most advanced, fully integrated public transport systems in Europe. Glaswegians are very reliant on buses, the underground, and the local railway network that cross the city and link it to the suburbs. The Travel Centre at St Enoch Square (+44 (0) 141 226 4826) is a good starting-point for general Glasgow-wide travel information.



Underground: The Glasgow Underground has twin tunnels providing a circular service linking 15 stations. Trains are frequent (approximately one every five minutes), and tickets are cheap, but must be bought in advance of the journey either from a machine or from the ticket office at the station.



Rail: An extensive network of regular and frequent local rail services operates in Glasgow, its suburbs and the West of Scotland. Most suburbs and towns within this area are accessible by train. If you are staying outside Glasgow and do not want to drive into the city, you can use the 'Park-and-Ride' system, which allows free parking at suburban rail stations, where you then continue your journey by train. CCTV protects most of the parking facilities - the Park-and-Ride also operates at four Glasgow Underground stations.

<answer>



Glasgow is the only UK entry in the list of top 10 cities in the world in the 2009 Lonely Planet Guide.



It is known for its spacious, relaxing parks – there are over 90 parks & green areas within the City boundaries, more than any other city its size. Many contain some of the city’s main galleries & attractions, and facilities for recreational activities.
FREE Ranger-led guided walks are available at Botanic Gardens,Glasgow Green & Kelvingrove.
http://www.glasgow.gov.uk/en/Residents/P...



Art & culture are important in Glasgow life where galleries & museums are in abundance. http://www.glasgowmuseums.com/ All have Free entry – great for someone on a budget.



There's year-round calendar of festivals and events, many of them FREE. -see links



Good transport links mean you can get to most of the major attractions quickly & easily. The Underground train system is a fast way to travel from the City Centre to West End venues & several sites on the south of the River Clyde. A Discovery Ticket (£1.90) gives one day's unlimited travel after 9:30, all day Sunday.



Here's what I believe are Glasgow’s “must-see” attractions:



Kelvingrove Art Gallery and Museum
[nearest Underground Station: Kelvinhall]



The Museum of Transport
[nearest Underground Station: Kelvinhall]



The Burrell Collection, Pollock Park & Pollock House
Pollock House has the finest privately owned collection of Spanish paintings in the UK, including works by Goya, Murillo, & El Greco.
[Getting there: Train from Glasgow Central Station to East Kilbride. Get off at Pollokshaws West (2nd stop). Turn left at station exit. Walk down the slight slope onto the north side of Pollokshaws Road. Continue (east), about 100 metres in all, over the river to the first down kerb, (entrance to Pollok Park), turn left here. Or number 45 bus from North Hanover Street].



In the old centre of the city, Provand's Lordship (Glasgow’s Oldest House, 1462), & Surrounds (including Glasgow Cathedral, Necropolis & St. Mungo Museum.
http://www.glasgowcathedral.org.uk/
http://www.igougo.com/attractions-review...
[Getting there: First Bus services 11, 36, 37, 38, 42, 89, and 138 all stop near the museum]



In the Merchant City area, the Trades Hall is one of the most important historic buildings. Designed & built from 1791-1794 by Robert Adam and his brothers, as a meeting place for the city’s Trades House & 14 Incorporated Crafts. Today, the Trades House still meets in the building, which apart from the mediaeval cathedral is the only building in Glasgow still used for its original purpose. It has many unique features including the original Adam white marble fireplace, a seven metre dome bearing the coat of arms of each of the 14 Incorporated Trades, Spanish mahogany panels and spectacular windows. You can tour the building without a guide Mon-Fri & Sat. mornings or with a guide on Tuesdays. FREE Admission.
http://www.tradeshallglasgow.co.uk



Take a FREE 45 min. guided tour of the magnificent City Chambers building. Glasgow’s municipal headquarters in George Square stands as a proud statement to the city’s position during the 19th century as "Second City of the Empire." The tour runs twice daily - 10:30am & 2:30pm. There’s no need to book
http://www.glasgow.gov.uk/en/YourCouncil...



The Gallery of Modern Art (GOMA)
[nearest Underground Station: Buchanan St]



Glasgow Science Centre (including IMAX & Glasgow Tower)
[nearest Underground Station: Cessnock] - Exit station & turn left. Follow brown pedestrian signs. (10 mins walk)



People's Palace & historic Glasgow Green.
Here too, you will see the fabulous Templetons Carpet Factory Building, modelled on the Doge’s Palace in Venice.
http://www.glasgowsculpture.com/pg_image...
[nearest Underground Station: St Enoch, 15 mins walk]



The Botanic Gardens & the Kibble Palace: http://www.glasgow.gov.uk/en/Residents/P...
[nearest Underground Station: Hillhead]



Glasgow University & grounds, and Kelvingrove Park.
Whilst here, visit Scotland’s oldest public museum, the Hunterian, & the Mackintosh House. Entry to the Mackintosh House is normally £3, but is free on Wednesday afternoons after 2pm.
Or take a FREE ranger-led guided walk in Kelvingrove Park
[nearest Underground Stations: Hillhead & Kelvinhall]



The Tenement House - a 19th century Glasgow tenement house, the home, for over 50 years, of Miss Agnes Toward, an ordinary lady who kept all sorts of things others would have thrown away. (£5)
http://www.nts.org.uk/Property/59/Detail...
[nearest Underground Station: Cowcaddens , 10 minute walk]



The Mackintosh Ten (i.e. Mackintosh House, Willow Tea Rooms, Ruchill Church Hall, Queen's Cross Church, School of Art, Martyrs' Public School, the Lighthouse, Daily Record Building, Scotland Street School Museum, & House for an Art Lover). All were designed by Charles Rennie Mackintosh. Take the Mackintosh Trail – with a one-day CRM Trail ticket (£12). with unlimited travel on the Underground, Firs



</answer>

<answer>



Try to find the M8 & head east for 44 miles



Edinburgh Castle



A must see. Queues get very busy in summer so you'll save a lot of time by booking tickets in advance through the official Edinburgh Castle website http://www.edinbu...



Royal Botanic Garden



70 acres of beautifully manicured lawns and flowers: a perfect outing for a sunny afternoon.



Guildford Arms Good Pub



Museum of Scotland



Museum delineating the history and culture of Scotland from the Stone Age to the present.



Camera Obscura and World of Illusions A unique image of Edinburgh is displayed on the fascinating Camera Obscura. On the way to the main attraction you will experience a number of visual treats, including stunning views of the



Royal Yacht Britannia



One of the world's most famous ships in British maritime history can now be boarded for tours.



Scott Monument



A 200-foot spire dedicated to Sir Walter Scott, the famous Scottish poet.



Royal Mile



Stretch of road connecting Edinburgh Castle with Holyrood Palace, the Queen's residence in Scotland.



Edinburgh Playhouse A traditionally styled 3 tier theatre with over 3000 seats, offering a broad range of shows ranging from visiting West End performances, dance, musicals, comedians to events staged by local theatre groups..



New Town Bar Gay bar



The Cabaret Voltaire Good Club



Pear Tree House Good Student Pub



Cafe Royal Circle Bar Tourist bar



Greyfriars Church



Historical church famous for its cemetery where Bobby, a loyal terrier, kept watch over his master's grave for 14 years.



Holyrood Park



Holyrood Park, also known as the Queen's Park since it is still owned by the monarchy, contains some interesting attractions such as the remains of an ancient volcano, a bird sanctuary, the dramatic Salisbury Crags and three lochs or lakes.



Arthur's Seat



Located within the center of Edinburgh at Holyrood Park, the remains of this extinct volcano rise 823 feet making it a notable landmark of the city.



St. Giles Cathedral



This is the main church of The Church of Scotland.



Dean Gallery



Art gallery with an impressive collection of Dada and Surrealist art.



Castle Rock This massive rock towering over Edinburgh is the setting for Edinburgh Castle and serves as one of the most defining symbols of Scottish history.



Portobello Beach My local beach



Scotch Whisky Heritage Centre



Learn about the history of Scotland's national drink on a tour of this museum.



Edinburgh Hop on Hop Off Bus tour



Real Mary King's Close



A guided tour through a warren of hidden streets beneath the Royal Mile dating back to the 16th century: dramatic episodes from the past are recreated, including life in plague-ridden Edinburgh during the 17th century.



Our Dynamic Earth



Scotland's millennium dome and science museum, featuring several interactive exhibits for the whole family.



Leith My home area



Bow Bar



Small quiet bar with a large selection of whisky.



Museum of Childhood



The Filmhouse



A small, three-screened theater playing Scotland's most innovative independent films.



Butterfly and Insect World



Maggie Dickson's



This pub is named after a woman who survived the gallows, a punishment she received for allegedly murdering her newborn baby.



Old Town



Edinburgh's oldest neighborhood, dating back to medieval times: these small streets are lined with wool shops, pubs and historical monuments.



Craigmillar Castle Another castle in Edinburgh



Princes Street Gardens



Impeccably landscaped park and woodland surrounding Edinburgh Castle.



Jekyll & Hyde Pub



Fun, friendly pub named after Robert Louis Stevenson's famous story.



Princes Street



A busy street lined with shops, historic monuments and restaurants.



St. Margaret's Chapel



This stately chapel is the only original building left in the Edinburgh Castle complex.



Murrayfield Ice Rink



Want family-friendly entertainment that won't break your budget? Try ice skating at this rink.



Holyroodhouse Palace



This is an official residence of the Queen and the Royal Family.



Greyfriars Bobby Memorial



This memorial commemorates the loyal pooch who spent 14 years by his master's grave.



New Town



The modern face of Edinburgh, with lots of restaurants, pubs, museums and galleries.



Calton Hill



Historic neighborhood lined with monuments dating back to the Enlightenment.



Three Sisters



Large pub located in the Cowgate with an outdoor courtyard and a number of themed bar areas inside.



Hopetoun House



This historic building is known as "Scotland's finest stately home."



Jenner's



Ritzy department store selling everything from bedding to Edinburgh Crystal.



Charlotte Square



Considered to be one of the finest Georgian squares in Britain, this plaza has maintained many of its elegant, old buildings on all four sides.



No carpet factories I'm afraid.

<question from>



justmeru...

<question>



7 Hours in London?



A few days after Christmas I will have a 9 hour layover at Heathrow.  I've never been to London.  Any suggestions on what I should do?  I'm in my mid 20s.  Maybe a cool area to go?  One or two sights to see?  Should I take the Heathrow Express or the Underground?



</question>



<answer from>



Thomas Talboy, PhD
Member since: 10 December 2005
Total points: 1858 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



The London Underground from Heathrow (The Picadilly Line) is undergoing some major renovation, so it is probably not the best way to go.  Take the Express train to Paddington (depart Heathrow every 15mins) and from there you can catch the underground.



Visit the website below for the underground information.  Specifically look at the Picadilly line services.  You will see that they have worked out an agreement with the Express.



Since you really don't have time to visit the insides of much, you could do a lot by going to Buckingham palace and then wondering toward Parliament from there.  You can see the barracks, St. James Park, Whitehall, Parliament, Big Ben and Westminster.



You could go into Westminster (the front part is open free of charge, but if you want to visit the section with tombs of poets etc., then you have to pay an extra bit (worth it if you haven't seen it).



You could then easily get to Trafalgar or Picadilly/Soho and find a pub to visit.



That makes for a nice, quick, central visit.  There are other options too.



I am happy to make specific recommendations. I provide personal tours and group tours of Rome and Europe. Feel free to contact me through my website listed below.



Thomas Talboy, PhD, Director
Ubiquity International, LLC
Experience. Explore. Embrace the World.(TM)
http://www.ubiquityinternational.com



Source(s):



http://www.tfl.gov.uk/tube/



</answer>

if it says 8, then that means 8, no earlier, and no more than a few minutes late
shoes? some prefer you to take them off, some dont, take your lead from whoever answers the door, look at their feet, seldom will people expect a guest to take their shoes off, but fresh socks just incase!



as for a gift, it is polite but not essential, i would go for a small box of chocolates, i dont know where you are in scotland, but look for something like a thorntons, and get a small box of chocolates, or, if you can, something from your home country that represents you, it may not be exactly like you have at home, but there are many greek delis here
or, a bottle of wine from greece would also be greatly acceptable



please dont worry too much, the one thing we scots are known for, is our warm hospitality! we just require good company, not gifts



enjoy

<original file>2066.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk111263.txt</current file name>




<category>



Travel



United Kingdom



</category>



<question from>



??????
Member since: 27 October 2007
Total points: 9 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



Why is it said that it is a "Glaswegian tradition" to using "Red Sandstone"?



is this true??



and is it the same for edinburgh??



how about other towns in Scotland?  Ayr? (train station?)



what about in England??



btw, does anyone know if brick is made from sandstone?



Thanks for your answres!



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Glasgow used  both red and gray sandstone in the tenement building period of the 19th century Although red sand stone was found relatively close by ( Giffnock for example ) the more prestigious buildings were built of Locharriggs sand stone from Dumfries . Edinburgh too used sandstone whilst Abedrdeen was famed for its granite ( Ruberslaw ) . The lack of suitable stone  in England saw the traditional brick made from clay used for centuries . Portland stone in the south of England is their most important stone Many buildings in London used stone from Caen in France .



</answer>

<answer from>



Elmbeard
Member since: 07 March 2008
Total points: 4906 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



Motorways include M1 - North, A1(M) - North east, M11 - Cambridge, M2 - Canterbury, M20 - Dover, M3 - Southampton, M4 - South Wales, M40 - Birmingham.  The M25 is the orbital ring road.



Rail Terminals are Paddington (West), Waterloo, Victoria (South), London Bridge (South East), Liverpool St (East Anglia), Kings Cross, St Pancras, Marylebone & Euston (Midlands, North & Scotland).  St Pancras is also an International Station with rail links to France.



Airports are Heathrow (Middlesex), Gatwick (Sussex), Stanstead (Essex), Luton (Bedfordshire) and the little City Airport serving Western Europe.



The Underground Railway Network is a very important way to get around London, as are the buses.  Black cabs go everywhere and you see them everywhere.



London is hardly used any more as a port - the docklands were redeveloped 20 years ago for offices and luxury apartment blocks.  Most sea traffic goes through Felixstowe in Suffolk, and the Channel ports of Dover and Folkestone in Kent, Harwich in Suffolk, and Southampton and Portsmouth in Hampshire.



Source(s):



http://www.tfl.gov.uk/modalpages/2625.as...
http://www.nationalrail.co.uk/system/gal...
http://www.tfl.gov.uk/tfl/livetravelnews...



</answer>

<question from>

<answer from>



PabloG
Member since: 01 January 2008

</answer from>

<question from>

</question from>



<question>



Im going to glossop in the uk, whats there to do daytime, with kids?



any info welcome, i have no idea how big it is is it a town, village etc shops? any good places to eat?  thanks



</question>



<answer from>



Alison C
Member since: 14 August 2007
Total points: 4814 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



my family live in glossop.  there are your normal high street shops.  there is a nice outdoor and indoor market.  There isnt really much for the young ones there.  There are a nice few cafes to eat in and there is a museum in the town market square.  Oh also there is a lovely big park, that has train rides for the kids and a playground.



if snakes pass is open then you can go up to castleton and take them down the caves.



best time to go to glossop is when they have the victorian weekends and christmas fairs.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thanks



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Justin R
Member since: 14 August 2007
Total points: 1618 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Its a very small and quiet village on the Greater Manchester/Derbyshire boarder and has one of the main (infamous) routes into Sheffield passing through - the Snake Pass.  It's infamous due to the amount of fatalities on the road and because of the fact it's always closed.  It's very beautiful and picturesque though!



There isn't much to do round there to tell the truth, but there are some nice walks, little shops and there is a nice park to walk around as well, but it is very cold at the moment and i can imagine it will be worse on the Peaks.



Also you are close to the Peak Distict National Park and it's only a short drive away to the fairholmes visitor centre near the Derwent reservoir.  Here you can take a walk around the beautiful countryside, see and feed all the ducks (there are seriously LOADS.  The ducks will run up to you when they see you have food lol) and you can also hire a bike.



All this is just a drive up the Snakes Pass, but be careful driving up this!



Source(s):



http://resources.peakdistrict.gov.uk/pub...



</answer>

<answer>



Mmmm. Tricky one this. Haggis are extremely difficult creatures to catch. You could set up a trap, but this would hurt him. Or you could try putting some sedatives in his food to tranquilize him them bring him back home. The reason he has left in the first place is because it is the haggis mating season so you could try to tempt him back by getting a female haggis and the scent of her will have him (and other haggis') running back home for a leg over! Good luck.



</answer>

<question>



Moving to Glasgow, best place help!!?



Planning a move up to  Glasgow in a few months with my family, i want to be outside the city, where is the best town/village to go on the outskirts for familys but still close enough to travel in for work?



</question>



<answer from>



lil_tige...
Member since: 09 October 2007
Total points: 1105 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



kilmarnock is a nice place to live or stirlingshire bout 30mins train journy to glasgow centre 45mins drive



</answer>



<answer from>



mutley
Member since: 04 November 2008
Total points: 133 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



come to glasgows west end



id go for somewhere in dumbarton great for out door stuff and not to far from loch lomond if you really want to be on the outskirts



</answer>



<answer from>



catrina
Member since: 05 January 2007
Total points: 1630 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



Depends on age of your family - if school age kids then have a look at areas like Bearsden, Milngavie and Jordanhill (all north west, nest Loch Lomond) or NEwton MEarns, Giffnock and Williamwood (on south-side).



Alternatively, further out are Ayrshire (Ayr, Prestwick, Troon, Kilmarnock) which is near the coast yet commutable or Renfrewshire (Paisley, Houston, Kilmacolm, Bridge of Weir, Lochwinnoch).



Have a look at the local council www for school info.



Glasgow is a great place to live with easy access to the countryside and coast. Good luck with the move!



Source(s):



Born and brought up in Glasgow.



</answer>

<question>



Has anyone else dealt with a company called Extra Mile Logistics based in Henfield, West Sussex?



They owe us money for services provided and refuse to pay. They are now avoiding our debt collectors, does anyone have any ideas how we can get our money whilst staying within the law?



</question>



<answer from>



arbiter
Member since: 11 August 2008
Total points: 1688 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



What is the amount owed?  If it is over (I think) £750 you can apply to have them put into bankruptcy.



IUse the small claims procedure, get a court order and then :-



Find a bank account of theirs with some money in it and garnishee it.



If the amount is large enough get bailiffs to seize a vehicle or office computers.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Very helpful, we will look into doing this, it may be the only way to get some of the money back.



</asker's comment>

<question>



Ebay- posting within the UK?



If a seller posts from London to United Kingdom only, will this include Northern Ireland at the same price if not otherwise stated?



</question>



<answer from>



tweets
Member since: 27 February 2008
Total points: 3219 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>

<question>



Im mackin a meal for me mum HELP MEEEEEE?



well last night i was complainin about the food (as usual) and this time she said U MAKE  IT THEN follishly i said alright tommorow ill makke a meal ive got a make a meal AND A GOOD ONE not like beans on toast what do i macke and how do i make it ahhhhhhhhhhHhhHHHhhH



</question>



<answer from>



Sarah D
Member since: 03 August 2008
Total points: 229 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Ok! im gonna make this easy for you since it sounds like you have no cooking experience what so ever.

Things you will need: caned sliced tomatoes,skinny loaf of bread, cloves of garlic,1/4 stick of butter,teaspoon of basil,cup of cream,1/2 cup of cheese, and spaghetti.



#1. Buy pre-cut/ sliced tomatoes. In a small pot mix 1 teaspoon of basil in a pot with the sliced tomatoes. Cook at a mid temperature and stir occasionally.  No longer then 6 minutes.

#2. Buy a loaf of bread, long and skinny. Cut pieces of bread on a diagonal direction. Toast the pieces to a light crisp.



#3. Take 2 cloves of garlic and chop finely.  Melt down butter and mix with garlic. Spread over the toast.

#4. Take the basil tomato and put on toast.



#5.  Take 1 cup of cream and put in a pot. Add 1/2 a cup of graded cheese. This is your allfrado sauce.



#6. Make spaghetti. Boil the water. Then add the spaghetti until soft about 10 minutes.



</answer>

<answer>



Spaghetti is an easy, cheap and simple thing to make. You can even buy frozen meatballs to add to the sauce (I make my own sauce with canned tomatoes, just add spices, but for the first time you might want to use a jar). you can either heat the meatballs up in the oven or add them to the sauce and heat them together (if using a stove, make sure you stir often so they don't burn). When boiling the noodles, taste test them before you take them out of the water (some people like them softer than others).

I even buy shredded cheese (either mozzarella or an Italian blend) to sprinkle on top. And if you really want to impress her, you can add a salad and/or buy garlic bread that you just warm up in the oven.



</answer>

<question>



Why does the UK shut down for the night ?



Hi,
In semi-retirement, I am called upon at odd hours to sort out technical probs for the "military" in the UK.

Job done, I wander out in search of a decent breakfast.

Everything is shut, barred up, closed, knackered at 3-4 am

Not so in the USA. 24hr food & drink.

I know it is a case of lack of demand for stuff, but is a Kentucky Fried Chicken thing at midnight too much to ask for ?

We don't all work 9~5

Bob



</question>



<answer from>



Nick G
Member since: 12 May 2007
Total points: 1692 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



UK culture is changing.  There is more available in the night and on Sundays.

I remember when supermarkets were closed on Sundays - and when supermarkets started opening 24 hrs.  I'm not an old fuddy duddy either - just 28yo!

We now have 24hr drinking in many areas (few Conservative-controlled councils, though).

If you're in Central London, there are lots of places to eat and drink throughout the night.  In fact, Central London is clearly a 24 hour city.

I used to drive buses at night (9pm-5am) and I used to find some takeaways to get food during the night - open mainly for people leaving pubs.

However, in some areas including countryside, suburban areas and smaller towns, there are often more limited options.  You are right that this is supply and demand.  Businesses are businesses, not charities.  They do not have to stay open if there are no customers.

However, there is usually something.  Look for a 24 hour supermarket - they are in many areas of the UK and offer hot food to take away (chickens, curries, pizzas, etc.).  Try a service station which will often have pasties, etc. and a microwave to warn them up in.

In larger towns and cities there are 24 hour McDonalds, Burger King, KFC and independent fast food outlets.

Or take a flask of soup or stew from home.

Good luck finding your food!

Nick
:)



</answer>

<answer>



A lot of it will depend on where you live. A big city or tourist resort will probably have such facilities available around the clock, but I suspect there would be much call for it in a small village in rural England.

The real answer to your question is one of basic economics. Where there is a demand, there will be a supply. If there were thousands of semi-retired people wandering the streets in the early hours of the morning looking for a decent breakfast after sorting out someone's problems, I suspect someone would tap into the potential.

Most people, I suspect, would just go home and cook their own breakfast.



</answer>

Hi Bob - UK is still very much a "Puritan" country, inspite of the female navals on display.  Thus shops tend to close up at about 5.30 to 7pm.  This includes cafes, many of which are only open during the hours 7am - 5.30pm.  The last orders in most restaurants is about 10.30pm.

I say UK is "Puritan" because most folk still hold to the belief that anyone wondering about at 2 and 3am in the morning must be up to no good.

Until quite recently, it was almost impossible to do any serious shopping here in UK on a Sunday and this was due to a powerful group of Christians called "The Lords Day Observance Society".  Very Puritan, which had many supporters in the House of Commons.

It was really only during the "Elective Dictatorship" of Margaret Thatcher, that Sunday trading began happening.

I'll explain England like this.  In the 17thC The Lord Protector Oliver Cromwell MP banned Christmas.

The English as a result, did not really celebrate Christmas until the mid-19thC when Charles Dicken's book "Christmas Carol" was first published.

The Christmas we have here in England today is based almost entirely upon the "Christmas Carol" ["God Bless Everyone etc"].  Add to this a guy called Santa Clause, who was called Father Christmas until the Coca Cola company first introduced us to Coke back in c1916 or whenever it was the Americans joined us in WW-One.

The Santa Clause we have today is entirely thanks to the Coca Cola Company.
http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=j4WU0mlZB...
. . . .no son, I gotta tell you the truth, there ain't no fath. . . .

There is one American who still thinks of working from 9 to 5
http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mpKAA2VxW...
Miss Dolly Parton

If you stop in UK long enough, you'll be converted slowly to become one of us - a born again sceptic, unbeliever and heretic.  You will cease to be a Roman and instead will become like us, a Greek.

<answer>



I used to work at Tescos. Usually you are not allowed to work more than eight hours straight, however I don't know how much you will get for 11 hours, but in an 8 hour shift you get 15mins, half an hour for lunch, and then another 15 mins. So I reckon for 11 hours you will get another 15 mins on top of that. Ask your line manager or manager they will tell you exactly what you will get as they will have it on there rota for the day.



</answer>

It's not true. Can be just a little more complicated though since if the diner never intended to pay then that is a criminal offence (theft) and the police will prosecute. If the meal was just so poor that it couldn't be eaten and the diner refused to pay then he/she would leave contact details as a mark of good faith and the restaurant could pursue the claim through the civil courts. Unless other diners could support the claim of poor food then the diner would almost certainly lose and finish up having to pay for the food and court costs.
Don't try it. Either way you will finish up in court.

PLEASE RATE IT
.
I know about Sawbridgeworth.
Please read question thanks.



</additional details>



<answer from>



Silly Jilly ♣
Member since: 22 April 2008
Total points: 3803 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Trivia



Edinburgh



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



Sawbridgeworth is a small, mainly residential, town in Hertfordshire on the Essex border, situated between Bishop's Stortford and Harlow.



Location
Sawbridgeworth is four miles south of Bishop's Stortford, twelve miles east of Hertford and nine miles north of Epping. It lies on the A1184 and has a railway station that links to Liverpool Street station. The river Stort flows through the east of the town, past the Maltings (now a small business and residential area).

Nearby towns and cities: Bishop's Stortford, Harlow

Nearby villages: High Wych, Spellbrook, Much Hadham



History
Prior to the Norman Conquest, most of the area was owned by the Saxon Angmar the Staller.

The Manor of "Sabrixteworde" (one of the many spellings previously associated with the town) was recorded in the Domesday Book. After the Battle of Hastings it was granted to Geoffrey de Mandeville by William the Conqueror. Local notables have included Sir John Leventhorpe, who was an executor of King Henry V's will, and Anne Boleyn, who was given the Pishiobury/Pishobury estate, located to the south of the town.

Much of the picturesque town centre is a conservation area; many of the buildings date from the Tudor, Stuart, and Georgian periods. Great St. Mary's church was originally built in the 13th century (although a church on the site existed in Saxon times) and includes a Tudor tower containing a clock bell (1664) and eight ringing bells, the oldest of which dates from 1749.[1] Ralph Jocelyn of Hyde Hall, who was twice Lord Mayor of London in the 15th century, is buried here; images of many of his family and other locals have been engraved on brass, and hence the church is popular for enthusiasts of brass rubbing.

The town's prosperity came from the local maltings, owned by George Fawbert and John Barnard; in 1839 they set up the Fawbert and Barnard charity to fund local children and their education, funding a local infant school that still exists today.

Apart from the historic nature of the town, attractions include local river cruises in the summer and two annual fairs held on Fair Green on St George's Day and the Feast of Saint Dionysius.

By the time of the Norman conquest, or soon after, Sawbridgeworth's rich farming land was fully developed for cultivation as was possible with the means available at the time: it was the richest village community in the country. It is, then, hardly surprising that many important medieval families had estates here. The land was divided amongst them, into a number of manors or distinct estates; the Lord of each manor had rights not only over this land but also over the people who farmed it. The number of manors increased during the Middle Ages, by a process of subinfeudation, that is the granting out of a part of an existing manor to a new owner so that the new manor was created. Many manors sprang from the original Domesday Book holding of the de Mandeville family. The came to be called Sayesbury manor, from the de Say family who inherited it from the de Mandeville's in 1189. The many important people who held these manors built themselves houses with hunting parks around them; when they died their tombs enriched the parish church, so that today St. Mary's has one of the finest collections of monumental sculptures in the country.

The Lordship of Sawbridgeworth includes the following estates: Sayesbury, Pishobury, Gilston, Eastwick Hall, Overhall, Giffards, Shingle Hall and Tedmanbury.



Pronunciation

The name of the town is now almost universally pronounced in the obvious way, but this was not always the case. In the Middle Ages it is believed to have been pronounced "sapserth", and since then the pronunciation has varied to include "sapsa", "sapster" and "sapsworth", and even until the Second World War was pronounced "sapsed".

Current residents often use the casual name "Sawbo".



Politics and local government
Sawbridgeworth is administered by East Herts district council.

The local Town Council currently has 12 councillors, covering both Sawbridgeworth and Spellbrook.

Sawbridgeworth is twinned with:



Bry-sur-Marne in France (1973) 
In Parliament, it is in the Hertford and Stortford constituency. Since the election of May 2005 Sawbridgeworth is represented by Mark Prisk, a Conservative.

The village of Lower Sheering in Essex adjoins Sawbridgeworth, east of the railway station, along the Hertfordshire - Essex border. It shares the same postal code as Sawbridgeworth, although for local government purposes it comes under the Epping Forest district of Essex, with the Member of Parliament being Bill Rammell (Labour).



Education
Sawbridgeworth has a secondary school, the Leventhorpe School, which also offers a public swimming pool and gym. There are also three primary schools and one infants school in Sawbridgeworth.



Sport
Sawbridgeworth Cricket Club is one of the leading cricket clubs in the south of England, fielding seven senior sides and seven colts sides, from ages nine to fifteen. The 1st XI plays in the Home Counties Premier Cricket League, and the other league sides play in the Hertfordshire Cricket League. The main ground is Town Fields, situated behind Bell Street. The second ground is at Leventhorpe School.

Sawbridgeworth also has a football, tennis and bowls club.



Trivia
Notable inhabitants include Victoria and David Beckham at "Beckingham Palace" since 1999. 
The Leventhorpe school has a fully functioning and operational wind turbine, the first in the local area.



http://www.answers.com/sawbridgeworth?ca...

s



</answer>

Stephanie x

<answer>



I was a live in nanny and was paid £150 a week.  I didn't have to pay for food, a room, bills etc.  I was also told to inform the parents if I bought any food for the kids/used public transport so they could pay for that, but I never did even though I should've.  It was a 12hr day, looking after a 1-yr-old and a 3-yr-old.

However, I knew the parents very well, and that's how I got the job (I'm not qualified).  As I said I'd do it for cheaper than them getting a real nanny.  So...maybe £200-250 a week?  I know non live-in Nanny's can be up to £10 an hour!

Opening Times

<question>



Question about sending a letter.?



I want to send a letter I want to send but it is about 2mm two thick to be classified as a letter in the UK and instead would be classified as a large letter which is much more expensive.Would the Royal Mail care if I sent it as a letter anyway?



</question>



<answer from>



karenwyn...
Member since: 01 August 2008
Total points: 415 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



get it weighed propery, other wise the reciever may have to pay other end, and they add a handling charge on top,



</answer>



<answer from>



K B
Member since: 11 March 2007
Total points: 19210 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



They wouldn't care as such, but the recipient would have to pay the excess postage before it was delivered. And if they don't pay, your letter/item doesn't get delivered.



</answer>



<answer from>



yankiwi
Member since: 28 July 2006
Total points: 3910 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



You bet they would.  If you go ahead and post it without the correct postage then it will most likely be returned to you for additional postage.



If you do not put a return address on it they might be nice enough to deliver it to the receiver 'postage due' and make them pay the additional costs....



but most likely it will end up in the undeliverable bin and never make it to its destination, (without a return address, it wouldn't get back to you either).

Most of the mail is processed through automation machines and letters that are too bulky can't get through the belts and rollers and are likely to get chewed up instead of being delivered.

So you are better off to shell out that little bit extra if you'd like your letter to get there.  In a nutshell, YES the RM would care and you would be foolish not to pay the extra postage to send it as a large letter.  It can't be that much more money....

=)



</answer>



<answer from>



Kaz
Member since: 18 November 2007
Total points: 1118 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



If you send it as just a letter the recipient will have to collect it from the sorting office and pay the extra postage which is only 6p but also the administation charge which is £1.  Would they be happy to do this? if so, send it.



</answer>



<answer from>



Mark H
Member since: 18 July 2008
Total points: 1284 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>

It's not a question of the best school, most of them are franchises.

andyguen...

<question>



Where can i find the best authentic thai restaurant in london? And what about the best indian restaurant?



</question>



<answer from>



The Elegant Epicure
Member since: 10 July 2008
Total points: 1116 (Level 3)



Contributing In:



London



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



It depends if you're looking for something 'fancy', or a local gem.

You will struggle to get truly authentic Thai food in London as it is usually adapted for the Western palate and isn't as spicy etc. However, you can still get very good Thai food. Some to try are:

Patara (Soho) - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/5931.htm
Addie's Thai Cafe (known to be authentic) - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/525.htm
Esarn Kheaw - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/437.htm
Crazy Bear (expensive) - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/4147.htm

As for Indian, check out this list from Squaremeal:

http://www.squaremeal.co.uk/restaurants/...

You have to search hard to find local gems, but London's high end Indian restaurants are among the best in the world. I would recommend:

The Painted Heron - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/2613.htm
Moti Mahal - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/6243.htm
Benares - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/3646.htm
Zaika - http://www.london-eating.co.uk/90.htm

If you're in or near North London, then Meghna Hampstead is well worth a try. Their dishes have great flavour and they do Indian tapas at lunch. It's reasonable too.

http://www.london-eating.co.uk/34368.htm

Good luck and happy hunting! :)



</answer>

</asker's comment>

<question>



What is Coton like for quality of life?



How pleasant is Coton to live in? It seems very close to Cambridge yet cheaper than Cambridge.



</question>



<answer from>



Silly Jilly ♣
Member since: 22 April 2008
Total points: 3803 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Trivia



Edinburgh



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



Seem nice enough.

Coton is a small village three miles (about 5 km) west-by-north of Cambridge in Cambridgeshire, England. It belongs to the administrative district of South Cambridgeshire. The parish covers an area of 4 km². In 2000 it had a population of 720.

Coton has a primary school, a church (parts of which date from the 12th century) , a restaurant and pub (the Plough), a garden centre containing a Post Office, farm shop and cafe, football, cricket and bowls clubs, and a British Women's Institute group as well as other local clubs.

The Coton Footpath is a popular recreational cycle or walk from Cambridge to Coton. It begins in the city centre, crossing the River Cam, passing alongside the University's West Cambridge site south of Madingley Road, then crossing the M11 motorway, bypassing Coton Orchard and terminating at the village recreation ground. On the roads and gardens around Coton it is often possible to see Muntjac deer, descendants of escapees from Woburn Park in Bedfordshire.

As of 2005, the District Councillor is Robin Page.



Nearby villages
Madingley 
Grantchester 
Hardwick 
Barton 
Comberton



http://www.answers.com/coton?cat=travel&...

♣



</answer>

<answer>



Of course not! Placing a bid on an item on eBay constitutes a legally-binding - and law-enforceable - contract to buy that item. Why on Earth would I want with an amp - I don't have an electric guitar! I don't want to be the only bidder - because, then, I'd be forced to purchase it!



Just be patient! Maybe you have the price set too high.

<answer from>



diet_cok...
Member since: 12 January 2007

Contributing In:



Other - Internet



</answer from>



<answer>



NO!

Leaving aside the fact that what you're asking people to do is illegal, your reserve is what's driving buyers away.  Lose the reserve, and you'll get more interested buyers.



</answer>



<answer from>



monkeymo...
Member since: 22 May 2007
Total points: 1446 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



You must not do that or ask anyone to do it for you.

It's called 'shill bidding' and can get you into deep trouble if you do.

If any of your watchers are genuinely interested in your amp, they will bid when they're ready (most bids come in the closing stages on an auction).

It could well be that your watchers are just that - watchers who have no intention of bidding, possibly because they have a similar item and want to get a guide on the selling price, they might just be nosey or they could already suspect you of shill bidding and are keeping an eye on your auctions.



</answer>

<question from>

<question>



Hey I have an interview at Thomas  Cook tomorrow, has anyone got any interview tips? Thanks :)?



i have my first ever interview tomorrow and its for my dream job. I was lucky to even get an interview. Can anyone give me any interview tips that would help me?
Thank You x



</question>



<answer from>



Silly Jilly ♣
Member since: 22 April 2008
Total points: 3803 (Level 4)



Contributing In:



Trivia



Edinburgh



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



Wear a short black skirt, white knickers & bra & unbutton a few blouse buttons. Keep leaning over & crossing & uncrossing your legs. Always smile & wink at the interviewer. If you get the chance, brush yourself against him `accidently'. Works for me all the time. Good luck.

Seriously

What to do:
dress smartly, look bright and attentive, and speak clearly and confidently. Don't forget that only 7% of the interviewer's opinion of you is formed by what you say - the rest is judged on how you look, act and sound 
find out where the venue is beforehand, how to get there and how long it takes 
get your outfit ready the night before 
find out what kind of interview it will be so you can prepare 
examine the person specification and your CV/application form, and think about what type of questions they will ask you 
prepare answers for the main questions - for example, why do you want the job, what are your strengths and weaknesses, what are the main tasks in this job 
make about three or four points in each answer 
quote real examples of when you've used certain skills - just saying you've got a skill isn't enough 
take your time when answering the questions. Make sure you understand the question and take your time if you need to think 
sell yourself. No-one else is going to! Be positive about yourself and your experiences 
prepare some questions to ask at the end - use it as an opportunity to find out more about the role and the company. But don't ask about money or perks just yet! 
when discussing salary, know your market worth and start by quoting a little higher than this 
get feedback on your performance, whether you were successful or not 
turn off your mobile! Treat the interviewers with respect and give them your undivided attention 
keep your answers focused on what you can do for the employer, not what they can do for you
What not to do:
don't be late! 
don't swear or use slang words 
don't slouch in your seat or do anything else that makes you look disinterested 
don't smoke 
don't lie! The interviewer may see through you. Even if you get the job, your employer can dismiss you if they find out 
don't let your nerves show too much. A few nerves are normal but extreme nerves will affect your performance. Use breathing techniques and try to remember that it's not a life and death situation - there are plenty of jobs out there! 
don't be arrogant and assume you've got the job. Nothing turns off employers more than someone who is disrespectful and over-confident 
don't discuss controversial topics such as religion, politics and gender relations 
don't read from notes or your CV. You should be familiar enough with your own history to be able to talk about it unprompted 
don't criticise former employers or colleagues. Interviewers may mark you down as a troublemaker and a gossip 
don't argue with the interviewer, no matter what. Remember to keep things positive!
These rules apply for most jobs. However, employers in some industries can use more relaxed and informal interviewing techniques. In some creative fields (design and media for example) it may be expected that you turn up for the interview in casual clothes, as that is the dress code in the office. If you're in any doubt, do some research on typical interview technique in your line of work.

Above all, preparation is the key to performing well in interviews. Research the role and organisation, and prepare evidence and examples of your skills and competencies.

Where can I get further advice and information?
You can get advice on how to prepare for interviews from learndirect careers coaches. They can help you to figure out what employers are looking for and help you to sell your experience. You can speak to a careers coach on 08080 100 333. Lines are open 8am to 10pm, seven days a week.

Not able to get to the phone right now? You can also email an adviser or request a call back at a time to suit you.

http://www.learndirect-advice.co.uk/help...

♣



s



</answer>

<question>



When to pick sweetcorn



I have some sweetcorne growing in the garden that my grandaughter planted when is it ready to pick?



</question>



<answer from>



Broberts
Member since: 31 March 2008
Total points: 3465 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



When the stringy stuff at the top of the corn kernal turns brown, gently undo the leaves protecting the kernal and poke one corn kernal with a knife, if clears fluid comes out then its ready to be picked. If not then put the leaves back in place and check a few days to a week later.



Source(s):



Ive grown sweet corn



</answer>

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Cambridge is very popular with tourists. I suggest that you look up B&Bs and guest houses on these websites. Hotels tend to be very expensive for what they are, although the Gonville and the Regency are as reasonable as it gets. There is a Travel Lodge outside the City centre (beyond the railway bridge) and there are frequent busses in/out. If you’re in a car DO NOT take it into the city centre – the one way system is a nightmare and parking is ridiculously expensive.

http://www.cambridgeshiretouristguide.co...

TaraLoui...
Member since: 20 April 2008

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



To be honest, the selection of restaurants in Newport is rubbish at best so I would stick to Cardiff if I were you. 
Personally I would reccomend Nandos to you which is in the Brewery Quarter as the food is filling (great for lining the stomach) reasonably priced (most things are under £10), the service is quick and there's a little something for everyone with salads, steak rolls, burgers, pittas etc. Plus! It's very central so you wont miss a bit of the atmosphere!
If you have to go to Newport and like Italian then Victorios is lovely by St Woolos cathedral.

<question>



Reservation at argos?



do you have to be in a que to purchase a reserved (online) product at argos? if yes abt how long? can anynoe briefly explain the process?



</question>



<answer from>



ignorant fool
Member since: 25 May 2007
Total points: 1282 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



It depends on the store and the time/date you choose to go. Once I went to an Argos in South London in Saturday afternoon and I was waiting -not exaggerating- for 75 minutes. But if you go on a week day and in less busy Argos stores you just show your paper, pay and go to the queue to pick it up. All in all about 10 minutes.



</answer>



<answer from>



Kayla
Member since: 26 April 2008
Total points: 6614 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Languages



Other - Europe



Air Travel



</answer from>



<answer>



You can either go to the cashier and pay or you can go and pay at one of the pay-machines. In that case you won't have to get in the line for the cashiers.

Just pay at the machine (if you pay by card) and it will give you your receipt and then you just go to the pick-up point and wait for your number to be called. Waiting time could be anything from a minute to...apparently...75 minutes (yikes!)



</answer>



<answer from>



BRIAN C
Member since: 06 June 2007
Total points: 12459 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



The only thing that making a reservation does for you is to ensure that they actually have the item that you want in the store of your choice.

The rest of the process is exactly the same - you either go to a cashier - give them your reservation number - then pay and queue for collection OR you use the automatic machine - put in your reservation number and pay and then queue for collection.

basically reserving changes nothing in store you still do exactly the same as no reservation - except that your reservation guarantees the store has what you want - so depending on the day and the time you could queue for a few minutes or an hour.

..



Source(s):



done it many times



</answer>

Paco

Contributing In:

</answer from>



<answer>



Since you asked this question in the UK section, I have heard of the approx 120 mile corridor that crosses Great Britain from Hull in the East, to Liverpool in the West referred to as the "City of the North". Boosters believe that this heartland has the potential to became one of the great economic centers of Europe. In a similar manner, Dublin went  in a single generation from one of the poorest cities in Western Europe, to one of the richest cities in the world. 
----------------------------
Population figures:
Greater Manchester Urban Area - 2.24 million
West Yorkshire Urban Area - 1.50 million
----------------------------
Currently it is a 135 minute train trip from Manchester to London. A planned high speed rail would cut this trip to 85 minutes and make the city of Manchester more accessible to Paris and Brussels via good connections to high speed rail in London.



Source(s):



http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/uk_news/engla...



</answer>

<answer>



They usually advertise their vacancies on their website, usually in early spring in advance of the opening for the summer season.
Or you could just look for a "contact us" link on the website, and ask!

EDIT - like I said, look for their contact details and just send an email, or a letter, or phone them ,and ask how they recruit!! It shows some initiative and if you send a CV too, they may keep your details on file and remember you when they are looking for staff.

<question>



What the heck has happened to the south of england?!?



I moved back to just south of London (near Croydon) a few months ago from abroad and keep hearing horror stories of stabbings and shootings and people beating each other up and getting bottled and mugged and hit in the faces with crow bars... is it really that bad? has it always been this bad and I just forgot? and why is it this bad? I'm scared and really feel like moving away again.



</question>



<answer from>



Carrie S
Member since: 12 October 2006
Total points: 12313 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



If you are worried about this then ask yourself the following questions.  If you answer yes to any of them then you are at risk:

Are you in a gang?
Do you live on a large council estate?
Do you take or deal drugs?
Do you carry a weapon yourself?
Do you wear a hood and baseball cap?
Do you hang around on the street with no purpose?

You will find that 99% of all these crimes are inflicted on people who would answer yes to most of the questions above.  For a normal adult going about their business and making sure they take taxis home at night you are absolutely fine.  I live in SE London (West Norwood) and have never had so much as an encounter because I'm sensible.  The majority of people round here are lovely and friendly and I like the leafy area and small village feel of the suburbs.



</answer>

<question>



Which do u prefer more? manchester or liverpool?



i dont know which one?? to live in and have a great time??



</question>



<answer from>



Glen
Member since: 19 November 2007
Total points: 875 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Liverpool.

It's almost as large as Manchester the city (not to be confused with Greater Manchester which equals the size of Merseyside, not Liverpool)

It has a compact city centre which is easy to explore on foot, the new Liverpool One shopping area, Albert Dock and Cavern boutique shops.

It has a beautiful dock and waterfront area in the city centre, in fact the waterfront is a world heritage site.

It has quaint village suburbs such as Woolton, West Derby, Crosby and perhaps Southport which isn't too far away.

It has the only Tate modern art gallery outside of London, the most Georgian architecture and more green parkland than any other city outside the capital (the latter two a legacy of it's status as the British Empire's second city in the 19th Century)

And most importantly of all, it has it's simple-living jovial and quick-witted people who live for the moment and are fiercely proud of their city despite its oft-publicised shortcomings.

Finally, Manchester also has many good points (it is probably Britain's second city nowadays) and I've had happy years living in both cities so feel free to make your own decision. The previous poster criticised Liverpool unnecessarily based on opinion rather than fact so I write this to balance that opinion a little.



Source(s):



I have lived in both cities and currently live in Liverpool.



</answer>

<question>



Why can't all british speak like roger moore?



he sounds so proper and intelligent.



</question>



<answer from>



david f
Member since: 22 May 2008
Total points: 3289 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Why would we want to? Regional accents are a reflection of the origins and diversity of the different parts of the British Isles. The perception that Received English represents intelligence and authority on the part of the speaker died out about 50 years ago. Nowadays, speaking like Roger Moore is more likely to get you derided as a 'toff'. Perhaps the pendulum has swung a little too far the other way in this respect, but Britain would be a far duller and more boring place if everyone spoke with the same accent.



</answer>

</asker's comment>

Dear Lauren,



It's not a question so much about the heart of London as it is really small towns put together in one city. The neighbourhood best for you is one that is suited to your personality and budget. The centre of London is what is known as Zone 1 on the Transport for London Tube Map but many nice areas are in outer zones and easily assesible by train.Go to wikipedia and look up London and search different London Neighborhoods, particularly those in Zones 1 and 2 on the TFL map. I would go to www.gumtree.com to get a sense of prices for flats, afterward. We don't commonly do loft style flats here but there are many different sorts and prices.

<answer from>



Paco
Member since: 22 April 2008
Total points: 9751 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Royalty



</answer from>



<answer>



Edinburgh, Scotland is the best weekend trip. Preferably two nights at a minimum. It takes about 4.5 hours to get there by train. Possibly the most beautiful city in the U.K.
-----------
Best day trip outside of London is Oxford City. Incredible place.
-----------
Day trips near Greater London are 
(1) the boat rides to Kew Gardens from Westminster Bridge. Don't take the boats to Hampton Court palace. You have to go through two locks and depending on the tide, the trips could take forever. This is actually inside Greater London, so if you don't have the time for the boat, you can get there by tube. World Heritage Site.
(2) Windsor Palace is just outside of Greater London. Nice town
(3) Stonehenge is a few hours. Probably best to take a bus.
(4) Greenwich and the Thames barriers. You can get there by docklands light rail.
---------------------------------
Flights from Stansted to Dublin airport are usually about 100 pounds round trip. The train is about 45 minutes from central London and the flight is 85 minutes. Book you train with your flight on ryan-air to save money. Sometime significant discounts are available.
---------------------------------
There are some excellent discount prices on day trips to Paris (about 2.5 hours each way on the Eurostar. You could end up with 12 hours to walk around the city. I would consider this a priority.
First train London to Paris is 5:25 to 8:50 am (time change)
Last train Paris to London is 9:13 to 10:34pm (time change)
A nonexchangeable- non flexible day trip is 72 euros. You must return that day. The same fare as r/t child of 4 to 11.



</answer>

<question>



How far is Stratford London to Kensington London?



Bus, Underground please. Thanks. Please do not just refer me to a site.



</question>



<answer from>



helen b
Member since: 22 September 2006
Total points: 2287 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



If you take the Westbound Central line from Stratford Station, change to the District line  at Mile End. (One stop).
The Westbound District Line takes you through to West Kensington.
The whole journey takes about 40 minutes and will cost £2.50 peak time on Oyster, or £2 off peak (after 9.30am and at weekends).



</answer>

<question from>



Lisa-Jan...
Member since: 19 September 2007
Total points: 360 (Level 2)



</question from>



<question>



Does anyone know about buying flats? near manchester, england?



I am looking at buying a property and have come across some lovely flats, the problem is my fiance has been told that flats are notoriously hard to sell if you buy - we do not want to rent but only want to live in this flat for a year or two until I finish uni and get a nice house!  Does anyone know if my fiance's friend is right? Because he has really been put off the flat idea by this. x



</question>



<answer from>



redbeard...
Member since: 16 May 2007
Total points: 1912 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The way the property market is heading, it is probably better if you do not buy just yet. If you're only going to live in it a year or two, you are spending a lot on mortgage fees and solicitors fees in order to buy it, only to do it all again in 12-24 months.



And what if by the time you want to sell property prices have gone down 10-20% like some people are predicting? If the value of the flat goes so low as to exceed what you owe on the mortgage, you will be trapped in 'negative equity'. This means you will be throwing mortgage payments into a black hole of pointless debt and you might find it impossible to move or refinance.

There are two risks associated with buying a flat. First, there's the fact there is a glut of them in Manchester - surplus always makes for poor demand. There would have to be something exceptional about your property so that it stands out from the hundreds of pokey, cheaply-built flats out there which don't have a balcony and suffer from nuisance noise, traffic fumes and lack of parking.



Next, it is difficult to add value to a flat in order to make a return on your investment or offset falls in property prices. Unlike a house, you can't build an extension, refurbish the loft, add a conservatory or tart up the garden. All you can do is decorate or install new fittings which is pointless to do since most flats in Manchester are only a few years old anyway.

In a falling market, you're better off renting. Or buying a solid house with fixer-up potential in a good neighbourhood. My 2p!



Source(s):



I bought half of my flat in Manchester 3 years ago through a shared ownership scheme, paying rent on the other half. That means I want prices to go down, so it would be cheaper for me to extend the mortgage and buy the other half.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks for your help :D , you gave us a lot to consider there and  I think from this and the view of other people we will stay away from flats.  Thanks again! x



</asker's comment>

<answer>



The correct term for British currency is "Pounds Sterling" - More recently bastardised to to GBP (Great Britain Pound).

The symbol is £ not a squiggly $. The symbols are very different from each other!

You should be able to exchange your Dollars at any "Bureau De Change" but logically, I'd bring either traveller's cheques, or get the Dollars exchanged before departure. It's a lot handier that way - especially if you need a taxi at 2.00 am on a Sunday!



</answer>

<answer>



Squiggly dollars?! Are you serious?!?

Please, please. Don't make me go on a diatribe about why I wish my fellow Yanks would stay home and be quiet before displaying their stunning ignorance before all the world.

But I digress ... that so called squiggly dollar is the pound sterling (£). For the last few years £1=$2. So £90 would equal $45. And no, they will not accept US dollars in central London so you will need to get it changed into pounds. You can do this at a bank, bureau de change (which are at pretty much international airport), etc.

Seriously, the £ is the fourth most commonly used currency in the foreign market so it's good to know. You may also see this symbol € which is the price in euros.

I strongly suggest picking up a really good gudebook like Frommers or Dorling Kindersley (which you can find at the library if you don't want to spend the money) and reading up a bit so you don't look like an utter prat on the other side of the pond.

"some weird squiggly sign" ? Sigh. Yet another American ignoramus. You do your nation no favours.

Anyhow:



1. Of course it didn't give the price in US Dollars. Its a hotel that isn't in the US !!! Only the US uses US Dollars!! The currency in the UK is Sterling, and usually referred to as Pounds

2. The "squiggly sign" is this: £. It is called the pound sign. It works the same way that the $ sign does for Dollars.



3.The current exchange rate is approximately $2 to £1, but it changes daily by fractions of a cent. Any bank can give you the latest rate.

4. $90 is approximately £45.

5. No, they don't accept US dollars anywhere in the UK, with the exception of one or two extremely expensive hotels in London who are sometimes prepared to take payment in a major currency such as Japanese Yen from very rich clients.

<answer>



Hi. Your question made me laugh, you are so cute!!



Ok.. lots to discuss...

Swimming in the summer: Unfortunately, England is no where near as hot as Texas. So if you think you'll get to spend all summer soaking up the rays on the beach, you won't. During the summer, we have a few good days, and these days every one makes the most of. 
But yo can swim all year round if you want! It isn't arctic cold or anything like that, and theres always swimming pools!

School: Yes, usually schools end at the end of June, and start at the beginning of September. So you'll get 2 full months off for the summer. Most people here go on holidays in the summer, most commonly to Spain, France,somewhere like that, becasue it isn't too far away. For school children like you, most would possibly get a summer job until school starts again.



Tea: Tea is a very traditional drink in the UK. Many people drink it, but many do not. Me for example, I HATE it. Can't remember the last time I had a cup. So don't worry, no one will force you to drink tea or anything else you don't like. We many of the drinks you have in America.

British kids not liking you: Don't worry about this either. The majority of British kids will be super excited to hear that an American girl is coming to live near them! I think the American accent is amazing like most of us here. They'll alll love you!!



I hope you have a fantastic time here =]



</answer>

<answer from>



mine
Member since: 17 February 2006
Total points: 1267 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



I lived in England for 1 year when I was 16.  It was different, but not in the ways you are worried about.  Their school schedule is different than ours.  They get time off every few months, but don't have as long of a summer break.  Just like we get spring break, they do.  But, in October, they get time off too.  It was actually pretty cool.  And summer isn't shortened that much.  I lived just south of London in Redhill.  It snowed a couple of inches in the winter and was in the 80s in the summer.  All the kids in my school loved my accent and wanted to hear me talk all the time.  It was actually really neat.  I made some great friends.  They have some different slang words than we do and it can be confusing sometimes, but you just have to ask.  They will ask you what you mean when they don't understand your slang too, so don't worry about it.  And don't let all these people fool you.  They have just as many stereotypes about us as we do them.  And they will ask you about them all the time.
Somethings that are different than here is that people walk so much more there.  Although I suppose that depends on if you live in a city or in the country side.  They have a great transportation system and it will take you from one end of the country to the other.  So, you will get used to riding the train.  They drive on the other side of the road and the cars look really funny until you get used to it.
Another thing that is different is that where I lived the houses were much smaller than I was used to.  I don't live in huge house, but you will understand what I mean.  The average home size is much smaller than ours.  The same goes for the back yard.  I don't remember anyone having a pool in their back yard or even having a yard big enough for a pool.  They call the back yard the garden by the way.  Where I lived there were lots of town houses where the houses are connected.  Again, I'm not familiar with your area, so I don't know what it will be like there.
I loved living there and wouldn't trade that experience for anything.  It is hard to be away from your friends, but you will have so much fun.



</answer>

Member since: 13 December 2007
Total points: 1149 (Level 3)

Sgl

Total points: 274 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Kids in England don't drink tea.  They drink coke and fanta and stuff.  Things you may lose are: Oreo's, sunshine, space.  Don't see many Oreo's over here I'm afraid (but try chocolate hobnobs...they're amazing dipped in...well you don't like tea, sorry).  That's the thing though.  Start getting used to dipping confectionary in hot drinks.  It's something we do a lot of.  Sunshine is something we don't get a lot of.  But there's more in Bristol than up north.  It's cold and wet I'm afraid.  But we'll probably moan about it more than you do.  Also, house prices in England are ridiculously high.  So you will probably be living in a much smaller house than your used to and it might be terraced (connected to other houses on either side).  We don't tend to have pools outside.  It's too cold and just too expensive!
The good things you'll find is that wherever you are, you're always near the sea.  In Bristol you are near Cornwall where you can go surfing!  You'll probably need to start understanding the fundamentals of football (soccer...never call it this!  It's football now ok?!) as this is all anyone ever talks about.  And you'll have to get ready for some harshness.  People probably won't pick on you but compared to the Americans, we're not very polite.



Coming from Texas (bible belt I believe?) you will notice that religion takes much less of a stand in England.  We are a secular country and ideas of God do not come up in politics unless we're talking about religious clashes between different cultures.  Generally we are Christian - Church of England or Catholic, but most of us are atheist or agnostic.



I understand (but only through watching Rikki Lake...) that Americans dress a little differently too.  English girls are known slags!  We were too little clothing whatever the weather.  The age of consent (when you can legally have sex) here is 16 and the drinking, smoking and voting age is 18.  So you can do things earlier.  Apart from driving.  The age for that is 17.  But most people do ALL of these things earlier!



A few tips concerning slang.
Fanny = Vagina, not ***
Jelly = jello
Jam = jelly
Pavement = sidewalk
slag = ****
scallie or chav = "white trash" I suppose.  Often violent people who wear loads of sportswear and spit on the ground!  haha!

Have fun in England!



</answer>
