<answer>



Lots of people dislike it because it's a big city, and all big cities have crime and problems. It's a fact of life.

People in Brum seem to be under the impression that Birmingham is more important than Manchester, so they have a negative attitude to it anyway.

In my eyes, Manchester is a fantastic city; definitely far better than Birmingham. And I've lived in Birmingham for the last years, having spent the 20 years before that in Manchester... in fact I'm in Manchester now.

There's a huge student population, and there's always something happening. The transport system is effective and thorough (don't get me started on the congestion charge though...) and fairly cheap.

Housing is far cheaper than in London, yet many companies have their second office located in Manc. BBC Manchester, for example and the Guardian (founded in Manchester).

Lots of students are imports- they don't get 'beaten up for being a foreigner' unless they do that incredibly irritating thing where they try to pretend they've reverted to a Manc accent within the first week of living in the city... basically they say t' instead of the and think that qualifies as Northern...



</answer>

<question>



Sunday Trading Laws : Why don't Tesco's 'stagger' their Sunday Opening hours?



We have 3 Tesco's within three miles here. 
Why can't they stagger their Sunday Trading hours so as to get 'round the idiotic Sunday Trading Law restrictions?

One could open at 9 a.m., one at 1p.m, and one at 4 p.m (say).Then we could shop when WE wanted instead of when Big Brother says....



</question>



<additional details>



Thanks all; I'd still say why not stagger their trading so they can cover the whole 10 am to 6 pm hours?



</additional details>



<answer from>



GrantMit...
Member since: 15 September 2008
Total points: 255 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



because Sunday trading laws state that large shops can only open for 6 hours between 10am and 6pm (i.e a continuous 6 hour period from either 10am, 11am or 12noon) unless they are exempt.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thank you. I favour all 3 Tesco's near us opening at staggered times, to serve is (the customer) better.
Then we could shop 10 till 4, 11 till 5., or 12 till 6 pm at our choice.



Better still, why not have another Tesco just UNDER the size restriction limit, open 24 hours like the main one weekdays



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Scooby Doo 3
Member since: 31 July 2008
Total points: 2249 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



its something to do with the alcohol laws, no alcohol before 10 am on sunday(it used to be 12) , not sure why a shop can't open at 4.pm though, maybe it would be hard to get the staff to work then , IDK



</answer>



<answer from>



Fred3663
Member since: 17 September 2007
Total points: 10549 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



Restrictions on opening times on Sundays are now, under the Sunday Trading Act 1994, confined to large shops (defined under the Act as those having an internal sales area of over 280 square metres), which may open for six hours between 10 am and 6 pm.



</answer>

<question from>

</question from>



<question>



Is London a good place to live?



I would like to move to London, but im not sure if it is such a nice place. Is it?



</question>



<answer from>



edawg
Member since: 09 December 2005
Total points: 1076 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Voters</evaluation>



I lived in London for 7 years (I'm from the USA).  I think the answer to your question depends on what you want out of life.

London is big and exciting; there are great museums, theater, concerts and restaurants.  There is always something new to do and discover.

On the other hand, London is also very expensive, especially housing costs and going out.  The weather is not great, though the summers can be nice.

Another thing to consider is the people - I found Londoners very cultured, intelligent and they generally had a decent sense of humor.  On the other hand, they tend to be reserved (by American standards) so it can be hard to make friends - I think people tend to socialize mostly with their work colleagues or people they met in school (huge generalization there).

Hope that helps.



s



</answer>

STEVE S

<answer>



The police in Manchester are superb. I was on duty, in a hospital, on the day the IRA blew up the centre of Manchester and I was on full emergency alert ready to receive child casualties. The only reason there were no fatalities on that day was because of the absolutely heroic police evacuation of the area. I feel particularly strongly about this because my wife and three young children were in Manchester on that day and it was three hours before I knew they were safe. After that, I can forgive the police almost anything, except the behaviour of some of them during the Miner's strike.
Regarding people being fined for riding their bikes without lights and not wearing seatbelts AND speeding, I have spent my whole working life helping to patch up kids involved in road accidents. I only got to see the survivors, not the ones who had to be scraped off the road by the police and paramedics. Please just think before you ask critical questions about an organisation where the vast majority of officers are trying their best to do a very difficult job.



Source(s):



I have absolutely NO connections with the police.



</answer>

I've got to go back in November and I dread it. The last 5 miles of my journey into Manchester take as long as the 120 miles it takes to drive there and now I have to pay a congestion charge. Con being the operative word.

<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



As the UK people are not willing to tell you where to go in London, I a S'porean will tell you to go to the places of interest by tourists. I have been there 3 times from 1981, 88 & 99 and maximum stay was 1.5mth.  Where are you from?  To london for tour, study or visit.



As a 1st timer, take the City tour on an open-top double-deck London Bus to see all the important places to visit on your own time.  The travel up the river Thames by ferry to GMT village to see the Zero time point.  Cover the Oxford Street for window shopping, the Hyde Park on Sunday morning, the Sunday Market and visit the Kensington Palace within the Park.  Also must cover the museums, there are many to be covered, the Stadiums and the palace.



If there is time visit the castles: Warwick & Leeds, the Shakespeare's Village and the Lake District in Scotland.



Quick a pick for you.

Could anyone please let me know what the weather was like around Duxford on Sunday....(Cambridgeshire)?



Was going to travel from Manchester but the forecast was pretty bad!



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



Working on Sunday in Lakenheath, Suffolk - but just over the border the weather was pretty awful - rain from first thing in the morning until about two in the afternoon.



It really depends on what you were planning on going to Duxford for.  I wasn't aware of any airshow.  Nice pubs and restaurants around there - and you wouldn't have been affected by any rain!



Looking for planes?  Try the Rod and Gun Club at Lakenheath - lots of F15's of the base - but usually you would need to be there during the week and not the weekend.



</answer>

<answer>



At which King's Cross do you live?



Assuming it is the one in London, go to Euston Station,  jump on a train to Liverpool Lime Street where you can change to the Wirral Line of Merseyrail and travel direct to any of the many stations in Birkenhead.  Birkenhead used to have direct trains to and from London but the track was torn up and Woodside Station demolished years ago.



It is true that it would be cheaper to walk, hitchhike, take the coach, etc., but it is so (unusually) easy and quick by train by today's standards you'd have to be a real miser to consider these other options as they are so slow and inconvenient.

<original file>12559.html</original file>

<current file name>yqa_uk62168.txt</current file name>




<category>



Travel



United Kingdom



</category>



<question from>



momo
Member since: 06 October 2008
Total points: 96 (Level 1)



</question from>



<question>



What is the postal code for london?



</question>



<answer from>



ohh_doh_...
Member since: 28 May 2008
Total points: 733 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



There is no single post code for London. Postcodes in the UK are very specific - a postie can usually identify a street or place of interest on the basis of a postcode.



For example Buckingham Palace has its own postcode - SW1A 1AA



"SW" stands for South West London. the rest of the numbers and letters identify. area, street etc.



So you need to know the address that you are looking for the postcode for. Once you had the address, you can use the Royal Mail Postcode finder to get the postcode:



http://postcode.royalmail.com/



</answer>



<answer from>



BRIAN C
Member since: 06 June 2007
Total points: 12684 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



The UK post code is a string of characters added to the address of every building in the country which are unique to that building or that block of buildings/ part of a street.



If it is written correctly on each letter it allows the mail companies to use a computer to read the code and then sort the mail to speed it on it's way to the correct destination.



Therefore it follows that in the UK there are thousands and thousands of different postcode and if you want a specific one you need to tell us a specific address - London will have 10's of thousands of post codes.



It is also used by many other companies in the UK to determine specific areas - for instance car insurance companies use the post code to determine the risk in the area you live and amend the payment accordingly.



Also delivery companies use it with a computer to work out the best route for the delivery truck to avoid covering the same roads twice.



</answer>

#1 East Grinstead in West Sussex (East Sussex - when I was born !) ! Beautiful Place to live & it is so clean ! East Court Mansion - East Grinstead Town Council Offices - & the grounds surrounded it were bequeathed to the people of the town in 1936 ! 20 minutes drive by car to Ashdown Forest & 'Winnie the Pooh' Country & Ashdown Forest ! 10 miles (North) to Junction 6 of the M25 & 7 miles (West) to the M23 ! 20 miles to Croydon by car / train ! 30 miles to Brighton or Eastbourne on the East Sussex Coast by car ! 10 miles (East) to Crawley & 15 miles (West) to Royal Tunbridge Wells by car ! 30 Miles to Central London by car / train ! <answer>

Whatever you do, avoid Milton Keynes.

<question>



What does this Glasgow expression mean?



"Ma Scheme's gonny doo your Scheme"



</question>



<answer from>



Graeme
Member since: 23 August 2008
Total points: 1963 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



That would mean that one area of a town's inhabitants is going to "do" (as in beat 7 shades of sh*te out of) another area of a town's inhabitants.



</answer>



<asker's comment>



lol



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Carrie S
Member since: 12 October 2006
Total points: 12468 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



In the 60's and early 70's they built large ghetto type housing estates known as housing "schemes".



Ma is "my"



Gonny is "going to"
do is "fight"



So roughly translated into Engish:



The rather rough inhabitants of my underfunded area of council housing would rather like to have a bout of fisty cuffs with the inhabitants of your area.



</answer>



<answer from>



caroline ♥
Member since: 21 December 2006
Total points: 29664 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



It means that there is going to be a fight. A bunch of neds from one housing area are going to fight with a different bunch of neds from another housing scheme.



</answer>

<answer>



I can't think of anywhere romantic in England.



But some suggestions:



The isle of Mull in Scotland is really beautiful. Stay in Tobamory, and explore the mountains and Lochs around the town. It's beautiful.



You could always opt for London. It's a far more romantic city than people give it credit. Stay in a nice hotel, and go to some romantic restaurants.



Gower in Wales is really romantic. The Gower peninsular is just south of Swansea. Find a quiet B&B, and spend a week-end there. Visit the world-class beaches, and just walk along them for hours. It's always quiet there this time of year.



Centre parks seems a bit tacky to me, but some people find them romantic. I just think they're a bit too crowded to be romantic.



</answer>

They did a fabulous Lobster Thermidor, one of my favourites.
Your friends could do a trip over to the Isles Of Scilly, visit St. Ives which has 3 lovely beaches.
If they are going at this time of the year, then Cornwall probably has higher temperatures than the rest of the Country, and the Isles of Scilly are usually very mild.

<answer>



As someone who has lived in both countries, I think in general British people do have worse teeth, but they're not really that bad.  I think it is because British people have the NHS to provide necessary treatment and don't get any actual cosmetic work done, and won't pay for it themselves.  They could pay for it - as the Americans do - but choose not to as they have other priorities.  I think this is because Americans are more worried about their appearance in general - more Americans than Brits have plastic sugery, liposuction, etc.



But in terms of who is the healthier nation, I think Britain is actually.  Whilst there are people in both countries that eat awful diets, Americans to tend to eat far more fast food and to eat larger portions.



Also, I think British people do more exercise.  Of course in both countries there are kids that get home from school and then just sit in front of the TV, but British kids are probably more likely to go and kick a football around.  And, Americans drive everywhere - no-one ever walks.  Here, at least kids might walk to school or whatever.  I live in central London and only know one person that has a car - so obviously we use public transport, but we might have to walk 15 minutes to the station or something.



No, I don't think Americans are anti-British.  In fact, I think more British people dislike and disrespect Americans than the other way around.  In fact, many Americans tend to be fascinated by foreign countries and the people that come from them.  In general, they don't tend to travel as much as we do and perhaps do not have so much exposure to other cultures and this may make them a little ignorant, but you have to understand that being a little ignorant is not the same as being "anti" something.



</answer>

This give you more info..
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Telephone_n...



</answer>



<answer from>

Total points: 109 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



01865 221689



</answer>



<answer from>



Jones H
Member since: 02 October 2008
Total points: 242 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



Different areas different numbers, not the only one.



</answer>



<answer from>



Daniel R
Member since: 11 October 2006
Total points: 8657 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



Neither.



The first one is completely wrong for the UK, it's an American format.



The second one is valid for smaller cities in the UK. London however has numbers in this format:
020 8888 8888
020 7777 7777



(You might sometimes see this quoted as 0208 888 8888, but this is wrong as once you're in London you don't need to dial the 020, but you *do* need to dial the next digit, which is always either 8 or 7).



Other large cities have numbers in the format:
0161 666 6666



</answer>

<question>



What is there to do in southbank?



all the people at my school rave on about how great southbank is. what do teenagers do there like? 16 yr olds.



</question>



<answer from>



Simon Magnus
Member since: 14 April 2008
Total points: 4626 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



There's loads there; it's one of my favourite places in London.



First thing that's great about it is how eay it is to get to. Waterloo Station is nearby and that's easy enough to get to.



You got the London Eye which is great for tourists, plus great views of the opposite side of the Thames which make for some great photos.



There are loads of restaurants (including Giraffe and Wagamama's), plenty of street performers, and a giant green area which is great for picnics (where the London Culture Festival is held).



The Imax cinema is there as is the British Film Institute so if you love films and cinema, the South Bank has loads for you to do. And the Globe Theatre is about a 15 minute walk away from the BFI so you can appreciate some Shakespeare while you're there.



</answer>

<question>



Anyone here live in nottingham- i need help with public transport?



I have a flight booked on a thur morning and need to be at east midlands airport at 7.30am. All the hotels around the airport are really expensive so i though i could stay at a cheaper one in the town but i wanted to know.....
Is public transport available that will get me there for that time?
How much does it cost for an half hour taxi fare?
thanks
i want to weigh up the cost of both options



</question>



<answer from>



charterm...
Member since: 10 August 2006
Total points: 8146 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



There are buses from the city centre to East Midlands Airport. They go from Friar Lane, just off Market Square ( or if you are local, Slab Square).



It is not too clear if the service you want is F1 or that is the number of the bus stop that it runs from. Easy enough to check.
There is one at 07.05 that arrives at the airport at 07.39. If this is too late, there is an earlier one at 06.30 and arrives at the airport at 07.09. There are even earlier ones.
Be at the stop a good 5 minutes before its due so as not to miss it.



Source(s):



Nottingham City Transport/route planner



</answer>



<asker's comment>



thanks everyone thats great



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



g3mg3m.
Member since: 30 May 2008
Total points: 6430 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



I've done this before (travelled via bus to the aiport) and it was honestly ok.
I was considering getting a taxi but I'm glad I didn't waste my money - it was cheap and the bus was fine (on time, had plenty of room for luggage etc).
The bus actually drops you off in the aiport car park too so its not too much of a trek with you're baggage!
The buses are regular and run 24 hours a day 7 days a week (every 30 minutes during the day and 60 minutes during the night).. You can find out on this website (times and pick up location etc).



http://www.skylink.co.uk/nottingham/inde...



I caught the bus on Friar Lane by the market square (slab square as someone else above likes to call it lol)



Good luck and have a fabulous holiday!



Source(s):



I live in Nottingham and have done this twice before with no problems at all.



</answer>

<question>



Is Formby, near Liverpool in the UK built on a flood plain?



I need to know if it is , because I don't want to move to a place that is going to flood, a very old friend of mine tells me it flooded across to Haskayne , on a regular basis 35 years ago, please don't answer this without knowing  for sure what it was like in Formby all those years ago, I don't want to know what you think, I want to know what happened before the river Alt was tamed by the barrier all those years ago, also , why has Formby now been classed as a "flood plain" by Sefton council, I do know the water table is extremly low in Formby that does worry me a lot.



</question>



<additional details>



I just read Yvonne's 21 s answer,"she knows loads of people in Formby , who have never been flooded" wake up, Yvonne it flooded years ago, before the sea and river defence, now coupled with increased rainfall, Formby is a flood plain area, no matter how many people you know its what is going to happen with or with out sea defence, coupled with global warming and increased rainfall, I asked not what anyone thinks , rather what they know from the past.



</additional details>



<answer from>



Hayley 67
Member since: 06 April 2008
Total points: 5766 (Level 5)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



During the night of 11th and 12th November 1977 severe storms coinciding with high tides overtopped coastal defences throughout Lancashire and Cumbria. At Southport the midnight tide rose by more than one and a half metres (5ft) above its predicted level and flooded 110 houses at Harrogate Way in Crossens. No one was physically injured but floodwater up to a metre deep caused considerable damage to property. It was found that water had overtopped a length of sea bank dating from the 1890's which was lower than the other sea walls at Southport. Elsewhere in Sefton the sand dunes eroded by up to 20 metres (60ft), promenades, coastal parks and sea walls were damaged and there was much wind damage to inland property. The bank at Crossens was raised and strengthened during the following year and has not been damaged since.



As recently as January this year fields,Altcar,Hightown and Little Crosby were waterlogged with up to a foot of water as the pumping stations could not cope, Currently only two storm pumps are in a useable condition. It is hoped a third can be salvaged in time for the winter, while the fourth has been consigned to an engineering graveyard. Altmoouth pumping station is havin a multi million pound refurbishment to replace the pumps which control the level of the River Alt in adverse weather., also an extention of the weed screening scheme is being done.



A proposal has been put forward to have another flood plain at Lunt meadows.



Sand extraction from the dunes through storm surges and asparugus growing weakened the defences before Coastal protection orders came into place these were the natural protection from the water  and that is why it was declared a flood plain



Sorry if the answers a bit long!



Source(s):



Family live there



</answer>

</asker's comment>

<answer>



Unfortunately most of the big events for capital of culture took place during the summer, but there are still a few things going on. The Liverpool Biennial is taking place, 'Turning the place over' is still astonishing.
It depends what you are into, St Georges hall a stunning building which you can tour, or you could go and see a visual art exhibition at the FACT on Wood St.
The Maritime Museum is good and has a great Titanic exhibition, as well as the International Slavery Museum.
If shopping is your thing Liverpool One, The largest retail development in europe has has just fully opened.
The magical mystery tour is a good way of seeing the whole city, takes a few hours and leaves from the Albert Dock.



Unfortunately the Superlambanans where taken of the streets at the end of August and have been raffled for charity. There are four in the world museum in William Brown St, there is one at the cruise liner terminal, and it was a delight to see The Liverpool and Everton Superlambananas in the Home Bargain's shop at the firms head quarters off the East Lancs Road on Saturday.

Seafret1...
Member since: 30 April 2008

</answer from>



<answer>



North East Bamburgh and Seahouses are very pretty if you like seaside resorts, however, they will be bitingly cold and many of the attractions will not be open in January.  However, there are lots of nearby attractions you could visit, quiet places such as Holy Island, Craster (home of the lovely smoked kippers, a sweet little island), Alnwick Gardens or Dunstonburgh Castle. Embleton is just north of this castle which has a lovely beach, if you are prepared to wrap up and brave the winds!



Or you could go North West, to beautiful Cumbria, Cockermouth is a gorgeous little place and the hotels reduce their prices in January and February.  Or Keswick which may cost more, but, be just as pretty, they also have cheaper hotels.  If you seek a busier place than these quiet tourist idols, Carlisle or Penrith are larger and have more to offer - shops, bars etc.



I would suggest Scotland too, as despite the cold, they have a lot of all year round tourist attractions, you don't have to go far over the border.  Berwick is gorgeous, so is North Berwick.  Dunbar is also very lovely.  Eyemouth is a lovely little fishing village that has a fabulous pub called Churches, it's a hotel too, serves gorgeous fish dishes (as it would considering it's location).



I hope you find somewhere fantastic.



</answer>



<answer from>



SMI05011...
Member since: 13 March 2008

<answer>



THE PEAK DISTRICT! We normally have gr8 weather in January as we have lots of snow, not so much rain.



It has lots of great villages and smalltowns to visit these include:
Castleton - lots of caverns, air sports e.g windsurfing and parachuting, and old fashioned sweet shops and pubs.



Buxton - a few funky but stylish bars, lots of decent buildings, the devonshire campus, the royal pavillions.
Chatsworth - magic place!
Matlock and ashbourne - other example of brilliant trowns to visit.



However I live here and I work  for the council in the tourism sector so I dont know as much for other places. I would also recommend the Lake district, skegness, blackpool and Alton towers.



Alton towers has an incredible hotel and a brilliant theme park.



I hope you enjoy your holiday wherever you go.



</answer>

Chris B
Member since: 06 October 2008

<question>



Is Elephant and Castle a safe place to live?



I recently moved here to study at LSBU.
Ive read mixed opinions about this place
although the answers all seem rather dated
whats Elephant and Castle like today?



</question>



</answer from>



<answer>



It rather depends on your age and the kind of things you like to do. I think it would be safe enough for a middle aged lady who liked bingo, but some youngsters are going to be a bit vulnerable because it is an inner city area in the Borough of Southwark.



There are some racial tensions there. So, I would say go out in groups, avoid eye contact with the yobs, and ignore them.



Don't intervene in a fight, go a distance away and call 999 on your mobile.



I don't want to make it sound awful, but it is a fact that there are some very nasty types around in places like that.



If possible, why not move a bit further out, to say Blackheath or Greenwich, they are nice areas and quite easy to commute back to Elephant and Castle.



</answer>

<asker's comment>



The answerer, Morpheu. uses faulty logic; Having crossed it often, he BELIEVES  it to be safe.  But that's not the same as it BEING safe.
If he is on the bridge on the day that it collapses, he may find himself changing his opinion as he splashes around in the cold, dirty River Thames.

<answer from>



stepney_...
Member since: 14 March 2008
Total points: 1953 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



The current London bridge is classified as a super structure, it is constructed of pre-stressed concrete box girders, and reputed to be the safest and strongest form of structural engineering in the world.



All the bridges in central London are rigorously maintained and checked on a daily basis for any stress symptoms. (see the bridge trust).



Trivia: Apart from the United stated buying the old London bridge, thinking it was Tower bridge, the actual lights on the bridge are made out of Napoleon's cannons.



The albert bridge, (see lynx post) is the only bridge where as the soldiers from the local barracks, must break step, when marching over it.



The actual sign is on the old toll kiosk at the foot of the bridge (north side).



I won't go into the history of London bridge, no doubt someone will wikipedia it, i never.



Source(s):



London taxi driver, and historian.



</answer>

<question>



What is the currency of England?



Ok, I was checking the price of a hotel in London and for some reason, it didn't give me the amount in dollars. It says 90 dollars (per night) with some weird squiggly sign. What does it mean? How much is 90 squiggly dollars in American dollars?



</question>



<answer from>



CLIVE H
Member since: 31 August 2006
Total points: 45584 (Level 7)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Here in England we have the English (Bank of England) Pound.



If costs Americans about $2 to buy £1 (One Pound) approx.



So, if the hotel price quoted is £90 - that about $180.00



Have a nice trip!



</answer>



<asker's comment>



Thanks.  That makes a lot of sense!



</asker's comment>



<answer from>



Roy &lt;
Member since: 05 September 2008
Total points: 1275 (Level 3)



</answer from>



<answer>



€ = euros
£ = UK pounds sterling



≈ = approximately



Normally, items are priced in UK pounds Sterling. So, I'm guessing that it probably means approximately $90, depending on what the exchange rate is when you pay for it. $90 is a bit cheap for a London hotel though (unless it is a per person (min. 2 persons) rate as opposed to a room rate)



</answer>



<answer from>



Moonlight I
Member since: 03 September 2008
Total points: 541 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



The currency of the United Kingdom (England is not a country, it's a nation, and does not have it's own currency) is the British Pound (sterling).



£1 = $1.76 dollars so £90 would be $158



I would suggest, however, that before visiting our country you learn a little about it. You might then enjoy your trip to the United Kingdom more.



</answer>

<answer>



There are several large hospitals in Glasgow - and several more in the surrounding area.

You also have to figure out what working permits etc your mom might need.

Total points: 7969 (Level 5)



Contributing In:



Trivia



</answer from>



<answer>



730 Great Western Rd
Glasgow, G12 0UE
0141 334 2422



Set in the West End of Glasgow, Scotland, the Glasgow Botanic Gardens is a large public park with several glasshouses, the most notable of which is the Kibble Palace. The gardens were created in 1817, and run by the Royal Botanic Institution of Glasgow (founded by Thomas Hopkirk of Dalbeth), and were intended to supply the University of Glasgow. William Hooker was regius professor of botany at Glasgow University, and contributed to the development of the Botanic Gardens before his appointment to the directorship of Kew Gardens in London. The gardens were originally used for concerts and other events, and in 1891 the gardens were incorporated in to the Parks and Gardens of the City of Glasgow.



The site was once served by a railway line, and Botanic Gardens Railway Station remains today in a derelict state as a remarkable example of a disused station.



Get any bus going along the Great Western Road & get off at Queen Margaret Drive



Walking directions to 730 Great Western Rd, Glasgow, G12 0UE
2.5 mi – about 52 mins



GlasgowUK



1. Head west on Renfrew St toward Cambridge St 0.2 mi



2. Turn right at Rose St 0.1 mi



3. Turn left to stay on Rose St 98 ft



4. Slight left at W Graham St 0.2 mi



5. Slight right at Great Western Rd 1.5 mi



6. Turn right at Kirklee Rd 0.1 mi



7. Slight right to stay on Kirklee Rd 236 ft



8. Turn right at Ford Rd 269 ft



9. Turn right at Kirklee Quadrant Ln 26 ft



10. Turn left 154 ft



11. Turn right 394 ft



12. Turn left 115 ft



13. Slight right 82 ft



14. Turn right 92 ft



15. Turn left 253 ft



16. Turn right 0.1 mi



730 Great Western Rd, Glasgow, G12 0UE

caroline ♥

</answer from>



<answer>



The Botanic Gardens are located at Kelvinside in Glasgow’s West End between the River Kelvin, Great Western Road and Queen Margaret Drive. The gardens can be accessed on foot from Great Western Road, Queen Margaret Drive, Kirklee Circus and from the Kelvin Walkway.The Botanic Gardens is known internationally for its impressive glass houses and extensive tropical and temperate plant collections from around the world. A variety of themes of horticultural and botanical interest are found in the grounds as well as attractive walks by the River Kelvin. Its immaculate formal gardens and arboretum provide a welcome break from the bustle of the West End. You can get a number 66 bus from Hope Street or a 118A from Buchanan bus station to take you there. Hope you have a great time!



Botanic Gardens
730 Great Western Road
Glasgow
G12 OUE.



</answer>

<answer>

My boyfriend is a big LFC fan and from what I've experienced, everyone is generally very friendly and welcoming, the number of times you talk to random people in the street cause they happen to be wearing a Liverpool top, amazing. <answer>



I love castles and have visited many, I prefer the ruined ones that have seen some action!



Wales probably has the best castles:



http://www.castlewales.com/



I used to live near Pembroke Castle which is awesome, huge and well preserved.  Carreg Cennan is in an awesome location.  Manobier Castle is quite small but overlooks a lovely beach, great for day trips.



North Wales has Beaumaris Castle and Caenarfon Castles, both very impressive.



Cornwall has some good ones, Launceston Castle and Restormel Castles.



Scotland also has good castles, Culzean Castle is interesting as it is triangular.  Stirling Castle is very large and well preserved, worth a visit.



I'd probably go for Urquhart Castle as my favourite overall as the location is stunning with Loch Ness on one side and the mountains on the other.

<question from>



Samian's Sixth Account

</question from>



<question>



Was Scotland on the royalist side or the parliamentarian side during the English Civil War?



I'm writing a research paper on this and was sort of curious as to where Scotland's loyalties lay ))



They rebelled against King Charles II when he tried to introduce the Book of Common Prayer but supposedly allied themselves during the War itself?



I'm a bit confused on that



</question>



<answer from>



Chloe L E
Member since: 22 April 2008
Total points: 10157 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



Words & Wordplay



Trivia



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Read about Scotland's involvement here.
http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_Engli...



the Third Civil War became a war of England against Scotland. Here at least the English Independents carried the whole of England with them. Few Englishman cared to accept a settlement at the hands of a victorious foreign army, and on June 28, 1650, five days after Charles II had sworn to the Covenant, the newly appointed Lord-General Oliver Cromwell was on his way to the Border to take command of the English army. About the same time a new militia act was passed that was destined to give full and decisive effect to the national spirit of England in the great final campaign of the war.



Meanwhile the motto frappez fort, frappez vite was carried out at once by the regular forces. On July 19, Cromwell made the final arrangements at Berwick-on-Tweed. Major-General Thomas Harrison, a gallant soldier and an extreme English Independent, a Fifth Monarchist, was to command the regular and auxiliary forces left in England, and to secure the Commonwealth against Royalists and Presbyterians. Cromwell took with him Lieutenant-General Charles Fleetwood and Major-General John Lambert, and his forces numbered about 10,000 foot and 5,000 horse. His opponent David Leslie (his comrade of Marston Moor) had a much larger force, but its degree of training was inferior, it was more than tainted by the political dissensions of the people at large, and it was, in great part at any rate, raised by forced enlistment. On July 22, Cromwell crossed the river Tweed. He marched on Edinburgh by the sea coast, through Dunbar, Haddington and Musselburgh, living almost entirely on supplies landed by the fleet which accompanied him, for the country itself was incapable of supporting even a small army, and on the July 29, he found Leslie's army drawn up and entrenched in a position extending from Leith to Edinburgh.



Operations around Edinburgh
The same day a sharp but indecisive fight took place on the lower slopes of Arthur's Seat, after which Cromwell, having felt the strength of Leslie's line, drew back to Musselburgh. Leslie's horse followed him up sharply, and another action was fought, after which the Scots assaulted Musselburgh without success. Militarily Leslie had the best of it in these affairs, but it was precisely this moment that the Kirk party chose to institute a searching three days' examination of the political and religious sentiments of his army. The result was that the army was "purged" of 80 officers and 3000 soldiers as it lay within musket shot of the enemy. Cromwell was more concerned, however, with the supply question than with the distracted army of the Scots. On August 6, he had to fall back as far as Dunbar to enable the fleet to land supplies in safety, the port of Musselburgh being unsafe in the violent and stormy weather which prevailed. He soon returned to Musselburgh and prepared to force Leslie to battle. In preparation for an extended manoeuvre three days' rations were served out. Tents were also issued, perhaps for the first time in the civil wars, for it was a regular professional army, which had to be cared for, made comfortable and economized, that was now carrying on the work of the volunteers of the first war. Even after Cromwell started on his manoeuvre, the Scottish army was still in the midst of its political troubles, and, certain though he was that nothing but victory in the field would give an assured peace, he was obliged to intervene in the confused negotiations of the various Scottish parties. At last, however, Charles II. made a show of agreeing to the demands of his strange supporters, and Leslie was free to move. Cromwell had now entered the hill country, with a view to occupying South Queensferry and thus blocking up Edinburgh. Leslie had the shorter road and barred the way at Corstorphine Hill (August 21). Cromwell, though now far from his base, manoeuvred again to his right, Leslie meeting him once more at Gogar (August 27). The Scottish lines at that point were strong enough to dismay even Cromwell, and the manoeuvre on Queensferry was at last given up. It had cost the English army severe losses in sick, and much suffering in the autumn nights on the bleak hillsides.



Dunbar
On the August 28, Cromwell fell back on Musselburgh, and on August 31, after embarking his non-effective men, to Dunbar. Leslie followed him up, and wished to fight a battle at Dunbar on Sunday, the 1st of September. But again the kirk intervened, this time to forbid Leslie to break the Sabbath, and the unfortunate Scottish commander could only establish himself on Doon Hill, near Dunbar, and send a force to Cockburnspath to bar the Berwick road. He had now 23,000 men to Cromwell's 11,000, and proposed, faute de mieux, to starve Cromwell into surrender. But the English army was composed of "ragged soldiers with bright muskets," and had a great captain of undisputed authority at their head. Leslie's, on the other hand, had lost such discipline as it had ever possessed, and was now, under outside influences, thoroughly disintegrated. Cromwell wrote home, indeed, that he was "upon an engagement very difficult," but, desperate as his position seemed, he felt the pulse of his opponent and steadily refused to take his army away by sea. He had not to wait long. It was now the turn of Leslie's men on the hillside to endure patiently privation and exposure, and after one night's bivouac, Leslie, too readily inferring that the enemy was about to escape by sea, came down to fight. The Battle of Dunbar opened in the early morning of September 3. It was the most brilliant of all Oliver's victories. Before the sun was high in the heavens the Scottish army had ceased to exist.



Royalism in Scotland
After Dunbar it was easy for the victorious army to overrun southern Scotland, more especially as the dissensions of the enemy were embittered by the defeat of which they had been the prime cause. The Kirk indeed put Dunbar to the account of its own remissness in not purging their army more thoroughly, but, as Cromwell wrote on September 4, the Kirk had "done its do." "I believe their king will set up on his own score," he continued, and indeed, now that the army of the Kirk was destroyed and they themselves were secure behind the Forth and based on the friendly Highlands, Charles and the Cavaliers were in a position not only to defy Cromwell, but also to force the Scottish national spirit of resistance to the invader into a purely Royalist channel. Cromwell had only received a few drafts and reinforcements from England, and for the present he could but block up Edinburgh Castle (which surrendered on Christmas eve), and try to bring up adequate forces and material for the siege of Stirling an attempt which was frustrated by the badness of the roads and the violence of the weather. The rest of the early winter of 1650 was thus occupied in semi-military, semi-political operations between detachments of the English army and certain armed forces of the Kirk party which still maintained a precarious existence in the western Lowlands, and in police work against the moss-troopers of the Border counties. Early in February 1651, still in the midst of terrible weather, Cromwell made another resolute but futile attempt to reach Stirling. This time he himself fell sick, and his losses had to be made good by drafts of recruits from England, many of whom came most unwillingly to serve in the cold wet bivouacs that the newspapers had graphically reported.



English Militia
About this time there occurred in England two events which had a most important bearing on the campaign. The first was the detection of a widespread Royalist-Presbyterian conspiracy how widespread no one knew, for those of its promoters who were captured and executed certainly formed but a small fraction of the whole number. Major-General Harrison was ordered to Lancashire in April to watch the north Welsh, earl of Derby on the Isle of Man and Border Royalists, and military precautions were taken in various parts of England. The second was the revival of the militia. Since 1644 there had been no general employment of local forces, the quarrel having fallen into the hands of the regular armies by force of circumstances. The New Model, though a national army, resembled Wellington's British Peninsular army more than the soldiers of the levée en masse of the French Revolution and the American Civil War. It was now engaged in prosecuting a war of aggression against the hereditary foe over the border strictly the task of a professional army with a national basis. The militia was indeed raw and untrained. Some of the Essex men "fell flat on their faces on the sound of a cannon." In the north of England Harrison complained to Cromwell of the "badness" of his men, and the lord general sympathized, having "had much such stuff" sent him to make good the losses in trained men. Even he for a moment lost touch with the spirit of the people. His recruits were unwilling drafts for foreign service, but in England the new levies were trusted to defend their homes, and the militia was soon triumphantly to justify its existence on the day of Worcester.



[edit] Inverkeithing
While David Leslie organized and drilled the king's new army beyond the Forth, Cromwell was, slowly and with frequent relapses, recovering from his illness. The English army marched to Glasgow in April, then returned to Edinburgh. The motives of the march and that of the return are alike obscure, but it may be conjectured that, the forces in England under Harrison having now assembled in Lancashire, the Edinburgh-Newcastle-York road had to be covered by the main army. Be this as it may, Cromwell's health again broke down and his life was despaired of. Only late in June were operati



</answer>

<question>



What and Where is a place called Scotland? ?



Where is scotland and what is it.



</question>



<answer from>



rain's child
Member since: 28 September 2008
Total points: 659 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



Scotland is a country in northwest Europe that occupies the northern third of the island of Great Britain. It is part of the United Kingdom and shares a land border to the south with England. It is bounded by the North Sea to the east, the Atlantic Ocean to the north and west, and the North Channel and Irish Sea to the southwest. In addition to the mainland, Scotland consists of over 790 islands including the Northern Isles and the Hebrides.



more info here: http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scotland



Source(s):



wikipedia



</answer>

<question from>

</question from>



<question>



I am going away and flying from London Heathrow?



Im getting the train from Sheffield to get to the airport, which train station do I need to go to for London Heathrow Terminal 4??? Is there a train straight there or will I have to get a taxi/coach to the airport from the station?



If so which train station is the nearest to heathrow!!??



Thanks!!!!



</question>



<answer from>



BRIAN C
Member since: 06 June 2007
Total points: 12922 (Level 6)



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



The trains and the underground go to terminal 4 - the station is called terminal 4 .



Your train from Sheffield will NOT go direct to Heathrow - you need to get off your train from Sheffield in central London and get the London Underground to Heathrow Terminal 4 .



Heathrow airport has three (yes 3) stations - they are T1,2,3 (sometimes called 'Central) - - T4 - - and T5.  there is no need to get a taxi or a coach to the airport.



...



</answer>

<answer from>



Williaml...
Member since: 08 February 2007
Total points: 660 (Level 2)



</answer from>



<answer>



Either get the Piccadilly Line from King's Cross St Pancras which takes you to either Heathrow Terminals 4 and 1,2,3 or Terminals 1,2,3 and 5, or Circle, Hammersmith and City or Metropolitan lines to Paddington then travel on either the Heathrow Express or Heathrow Connect trains to Heathrow Airport.



Using the tube would be cheaper though.



Source(s):



www.tfl.gov.uk



</answer>



<answer from>



Dafydd
Member since: 17 January 2007
Total points: 3428 (Level 4)



</answer from>



<answer>



I think the trains from Sheffield go into St Pancras. Get the underground from there to Paddington and get the Heathrow Express (it takes 15 mins) to Terminal 4. If you think that's a bit expensive take the Piccadilly Line all the way from Kings Cross St Pancras to Heathrow Terminal 4 (it takes a long time as there are a lot of stops).



</answer>

<answer>



Walk to Kilburn High Road underground, head on the Bakerloo Line to Oxford Circus, then change onto the Victoria Line to Victoria. Either £4 for a single journey on the tube without a Oyster card, though about half that price if you do have an Oyster.
At Victoria get on the train to Windsor and Eton Riverside. (estimate at about between £10-£20 for a return)
When arriving at the terminus you will either have to walk towards the castle and pick up the Shuttle bus(which costs about £4 for a return) or hail a taxi(no idea on price)



</answer>

<question>



I'm going to London in May for a 6 days vacations. My question is Would it be better to get a package deal ?



which includes round trip tickets and hotel or should I get them separately?



(I'm in Texas)



</question>



<additional details>



I'm going to a concert at the O2 Arena on May 30th



Can someone please tell me names of any hotels close to the O2 Arena (London). Not too cheap not too expensive pls!



</additional details>



<answer from>



carina
Member since: 12 April 2006
Total points: 11597 (Level 6)



Contributing In:



London



Other - United Kingdom



</answer from>



<answer>



<evaluation>Best Answer - Chosen by Asker</evaluation>



if you've got the time and the patience to do your own searches you may well be able to get a better deal booking things yourself ... but there can be some reasonable deals ... why not do a bit of searching to get an idea of prices of hotels and compare that to the packages



the area around the O2 Arena is not where most of the tourist hotels are ... the O2 Arena is near North Greenwich station on the Jubilee underground line ... see square D8 on the tube map
http://www.tfl.gov.uk/assets/downloads/s...
most of the hotels etc tend either to be in the west (Kensington, Gloucester Road etc) or to the north of the West End (Bloomsbury, Russell Square etc) ... the most important thing is that you get a hotel that is within reasonable distance of a tube station and you'll be OK



if you want to search for hotels yourself, I'd suggest you use one of the maps, e.g. www.multimap.com, www.streetmap.com, or google, where you can enter the postcode of the hotel and it will pull up on a map where it's located



</answer>

James Bond

<answer>



first of all for hotels near the O2 arena take a look here:
http://www.milesfaster.co.uk/places/mill...



However take note that London is split between north and south of the River Thames so you really want a hotel on the south side. The Express Greenwich fits the bill, its close, on the south side and its part of a budget chain.



As for packages the thing to note is how flexible do you need to be? Packages normally require upfront payment and hefty cancellation penalties while a hotel like the express wont charge you till you check-out. (unless taking a cheaper rate for advance payment). The only way to compare rates between booking separately is to choose and see the prices. DIY is so easy these days that making your own package has a lot of benefits.



</answer>

<answer>



In Edinburgh, Armstrongs (81-83 Grassmarket, 0131 220 5557; 64-66 Clerk St, 0131 667 6056) stocks everything from 18th-century military jackets to spandex leggings. For added feel-good factor Barnados Vintage (116 West Bow, 0131 225 4751) is a boutique charity shop with a quirky edge. Godiva (9 West Port, 0131 221 9212) offers a bespoke alteration service on vintage items alongside collections from ECA graduates, while Herman Brown (151 West Port, 0131 228 2589) displays clothes like antiques with an eclectic collection of Day-Glo 80s leggings and classy 70s dresses.

<question from>

<question>



Places to go in edinburgh?



me and some friends are looking for somewhere nice to go for food and cocktails in edinburgh, but somewhere that is classy but cheap, we have looked at the living room which looks nice, but if we can get similar but cheaper then all the better. also going out for halloween where should we aim for???



</question>



<answer from>



Kay-- xxo
Member since: 14 September 2008
Total points: 151 (Level 1)



</answer from>



<answer>



The trendy pubs are on George Street



All Bar One
29 George Street, EH2 (226 9971 )
An archetypal example of a former bank becoming a trendy Edinburgh pub/wine bar. Operated by a large nation wide bars chain.



Bar 38
126-128 George Street, EH2 (220 6180)
Wall sculptures and attractive coloured lighting provide interest for the eye at this popular after-work Edinburgh pub, which is in an area at the West End of George Street among other fashionable Edinburgh bars.



Candy Bar
113-115 George Street, EH2 (225 9179)
Great atmosphere and a good place to visit on a night out at the west end of George Street Edinburgh.



The Dome
14 George Street, EH2 (624 8624)
Underneath the vaulted cupola of this grandiose former bank building is a large central Edinburgh bar surrounded by carefully placed seating and plants. Frazer's Bar, at the front of the same building, is decorated in luxurious art deco style, with wood-panelled walls. Famous for their good food in their Edinburgh restaurant area. Must be one of the most stylish bars in Edinburgh.



Le Monde



16 George Street, EH2



This Edinburgh style concept pub, is situated in a stylish building on Edinburgh's George Street between the Dome and The Edinburgh Hard Rock Cafe. It's an 18 bedroom boutique hotel which has 3 bars and an night club called Tokyo in it's basement.



Standing Order
62-66 George Street, EH2 (225 4460)



It's still possible to see the safe from the time when this large Edinburgh building was a bank. These days it's a spacious Wetherspoon's Edinburgh pub with no-smoking rooms, no music and no children.



Tigerlily



125 George Street, EH2 (225 5005)



Edinburgh’s winner of The Scottish Hotel Bar Award 2007 . Hang out in the ground-floor bar area where the glass windows look out to George Street.



Or you could try Frankenstein's on George IV Bridge, 3 floors, 3 bars and lots of music.



Then move on to Espionage, just along the road, its a popular venue for visitors, its free to get in and has 5 floors all with different music types. It's in Victoria Street next to India Buildings.



Whatever you decide to do, I'm sure you'll have a great time.



</answer>

Total points: 342 (Level 2)

<answer>



Revellers are being warned that the gathering in Edinburgh, centred on Princes Street, is strictly an all-ticket affair and that no bottles or glasses will be permitted through the barriers. Glasses and bottles will not be allowed through the barriers and will have to be deposited in bottle banks.



Arrangements have been made with off licences to supply beer in cans and spirits in plastic containers.



http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/scotland/5662...



</answer>
